opposed to just what is below the aircraft and provides both an audio and a Visual Alert if and when an obstacle is encountered in the direct pathway of the aircraft in other words H toss keeps aircraft especially low-flying aircraft-like medical helicopters from flying directly into buildings towers and terrain like mountains and Hills the helicopter also was not equipped with a cockpit took place on May 10 2008 just outside of La Crosse Wisconsin in the United States a short side note is that the year 2008 was an absolute nightmare for everyone involved in helicopter EMS in the United States as there were 12 EMS helicopter accidents eight of them fatal resulting in a staggering total of 29 fatalities in just that year alone these accidents and events ultimately did end up Gunderson Lutheran Hospital in La Crosse arriving about 2154. they dropped their patient off pretty quickly and lift off again at 2209 and head towards La Crosse Regional Airport located on French Island right in the middle of the Mississippi River in La Crosse Wisconsin they refuel and take off again at 22 34 hours headed back to where they started just about two hours ago now air methods was utilizing GPS tracking that would update the position of the aircraft every three minutes but for some reason after they lifted off at 2234 there was no further GPS updates and it is unknown exactly what path speed altitude the helicopter followed after that moment but the guy who fueled the helicopter stated that the helicopter lifted and proceeded east Southeast and added that the visibility was fair with a low ceiling and moderate rain another witness who was located 4.2 miles east Southeast of the La Crosse airport reported hearing a helicopter fly over at about 2230 while he was in a restaurant parking lot getting into his car he specifically noted that it sounded like a med-link medical helicopter which is a neighboring flight service and reported that it seemed to be traveling at a high rate of speed and was flying low he specifically recalled to the investigators thinking that it was not going to clear the Bluffs a second witness contacted the La Crosse County Sheriff's Office all on their own just a few minutes later at 2240 and stated that he heard a helicopter flying overhead when the sound of the engine disappeared followed by a loud crashing sound it was at this time that local authorities initiated a search but it was not until 2304 24 four minutes later the air methods notified local authorities that the helicopter was indeed missing but no one knew where to search or where the helicopter was because the GPS tracking had stopped for some reason now the fire department report for this search and rescue mission is absolutely nuts and I applaud the hard work and Ingenuity that all of these responders put in during the early morning hours of May 11 2008. the search area was extremely remote and was located amongst what the local communities call The Bluffs surrounding lacrosse these Bluffs were originally created from the Mississippi River as it cut through the Mississippi River Valley and carved out the land eventually leaving many steep Cliff ridges there are many Bluffs that surround lacrosse most of which are about five or six hundred feet above the ground and about 1 000 to 1200 feet above sea level The Searchers attempted to draw a line that followed east Southeast from La Crosse airport and cross-referenced that pathway with what the witnesses heard and saw they also attempted to locate the aircraft by tracking the GPS data from the cell phones of the med flight crew member but this was way back in 2008 like a million years ago right when cell phone GPS was in its absolute infancy in the GPS location was not really easy to obtain like it is today dispatchers would repeatedly call all three of the cell phones without an answer and then leave voicemails but the only way for a GPS location to be provided at the time from the cell provider which was U.S cellular was if there was an outbound call from one of the cell phones or every two to four hours and so it wasn't until o200 that morning that U.S cellular finally advised that they did get a location on the north side of one of their towers but Searchers were still unsuccessful then another two hours goes by and they did get a more precise location which did help Rescuers and the helicopter was finally found at 08 26 hours that morning sadly all three of the crew members died in this accident foreign ERS could easily tell that the helicopter impacted the very top of the trees on the top of the ridge line of this Bluff the right Landing skid impacted the trees all the way at the top of the bluff and was ripped off the main wreckage then came to rest on a descending Hillside downhill from the ridge line with the main wreckage coming to rest about 600 feet further down from the initial impact in other words the helicopter was flying along in its right Landing skid impacted the tops of the trees at the very top of the Ridgeline ripped it off causing the helicopter to crash 600 feet further down the same pathway while analyzing the wreckage investigators also found that the instrument panel that has all of the gauges and switches had been completely dislodged from its normal position in the cockpit upon closer inspection they took a deeper look at these switches that you see here which control the number one and number two engines otherwise known as the left and right engines these engine control switches have three positions and a switch lock the bottom position is off the middle position is idle and the top position is flight in really basic terms the pilot would lift the switch to idle during engine start and then once the engines are within the specified parameters he would then move the switch to flight and flip the switch lock over but on early model ec-135s this switch lock only locked out the ability to move the switch to the off position not to the idle position when investigators first analyze this instrument panel while still at the accident site they found that the engine number one switch was in the idle position and not in the flight position where it was supposed to be so seems pretty clear that this might be the culprit right well not so fast you see the ec135 is a twin engine aircraft with a FedEx control system which is basically a very Advanced computer system that manages the power and Fuel and efficiency of the turboshaft engines so these engines and their respective computer control units were sent off to be analog advised by the engine manufacturer turbo Mecca these investigators can tell very easily by just visual inspection that both of these engines had internal damage that was consistent with the blade still turning at the time of impact now turbine engines have a limit to how fast they can spin before they eventually grenade blow up the FedEx system basically controls how fast the engine should spin based upon how much torque the engines are spinning against which in most turboshaft helicopters comes by way of the driveshafts that come out of the engines and go to the transmission if the resistance or Torque that the engine has to create power against suddenly disappears then the engine will start to overspeed and spin way too fast which like I said will eventually lead to that engine being damaged and blowing up so in a twin engine helicopter the FedEx system will shut down that engine if the speed reaches a threshold of 114 percent knowing that the other engine is there to provide Power but what if the other engine also loses torque well the FedEx system will certainly not shut down both of the engines so the other engine is actually free to over speed itself a little bit but then has a mechanical Safeguard which on these turbo Mecha engines means that once the engine gets to a 147 percent the actual power turbine blades themselves will literally shed away from the engine in order to protect against the entire engine blowing up in some type of catastrophic failure so when they looked at the actual computer memory and data from this crash they found that even though this switch on the instrument panel showed that engine number one was at idle it was actually in the flight position for both engines at the actual time of impact how do we know this because the memory and data show that both engines simultaneously experienced an overspeed event with a number two engine experiencing the event just a millisecond before engine number one at the exact same moment both engines showed that they had very low low torque values which means that the transmission had become disconnected from the engine simultaneously when the aircraft impacted the ground once the helicopter impacted the ground the transmission was slammed forward and disconnected from both engine driveshafts lowering their torque values causing them to over speed and since engine number two experienced the over speed to 114 percent first it was immediately shut down which caused engine number one to over speed to 147 percent causing it to shed its power turbine blades and also shut down which is exactly how both engines were designed to perform now you go back 45 seconds and watch and listen to that one again but wait then how did this switch on the instrument panel end up in the idle position well unknown at the time of the accident at least to the NTSB and air methods was Eurocopter had been experiencing problems with the function of this switch you see this switch is a three position switch where you actually have to pull on the switch out from the instrument panel in order to release a small triangular protrusion from the bottom of the switching lever that fits into a little Groove you pull the switch out move it to the desired position and then let it go and the little protrusion will sort of Lock and hold the switch lever in place but the problem is this little triangular protrusion and the groove that it fits into tend to get worn out by not being properly operated and not pulling out the lever all the way when a pilot moves this switch up or down without pulling the lever out all the way the groove gets worn down and as Eurocopter says under these circumstances a secure and exact positioning of the switch lever cannot be insured so Eurocopter issued a service bullet in two years after this crash that provides two fixes for this problem one it replaces the switch with a sturdier design that won't get worn down as easily and two the little red switch lock which normally would have locked out just the off position is now double in size and locks out both the off and idle positions now air methods as is their right also did their very own investigation which was quite interesting and has some very controversial points now one of the engine ecu's was severely damaged in this accident and was found to have a single bent pin in one of the connectors this pin just happened to also be the same pin that controls the overspeed 30 second indication lamp in the cockpit which would illuminate if one of the engines was shut down but there is obviously some discrepancy with this pin because turbo Mecca first stated that this pin controls the training mode lamp on the instrument panel yet the air methods report says that this was a mistake and turbo Mecca then retracted that statement and acknowledged that it was actually for the oei overspeed indicator but I don't think that it really matters Marcus Williams fastened his seat belt as flight 447 prepared for departure from Chicago. He was heading home to Atlanta after a grueling week of medical conferences. As a cardiothoracic surgeon, Marcus had just presented his groundbreaking research on minimally invasive heart procedures. His presentation had been wellreceived, and he felt a quiet sense of accomplishment as he settled into seat. One 2A. The middle-aged woman beside him smiled politely before returning to her magazine. Marcus pulled out his tablet, planning to review patient files during the 3-hour flight. He had surgeries scheduled for Monday morning and he wanted to be prepared. The plane filled quickly with business travelers, families, and college students heading back from spring break. Marcus barely noticed the bustle around him as he focused on his work. He had learned long ago to find peace in the chaos of airports and airplanes. It was part of the job, part of the life he had built through years of sacrifice and dedication. The plane shuddered violently and oxygen masks dropped from the overhead compartments. Passengers screamed as the aircraft lurched to one side. Marcus grabbed the armrests, his medical training kicking in immediately. He scanned the cabin, assessing for injuries, looking for anyone who might need immediate help. They would need to make an emergency landing in Indianapolis. 20 minutes out, the cabin erupted in panicked voices, crying children and frantic prayers. Marcus helped the woman beside him secure her mask properly, then checked on the elderly man across the aisle, who looked like he might be having a heart attack. Marcus identified himself as a doctor, asked the man about his symptoms, and instructed a flight attendant to bring the medical kit. He took the man's pulse, asked about chest pain, shortness of breath, any history of heart problems. The man nodded weakly, clutching his chest. Marcus worked quickly, administering aspirin from the medical kit, monitoring the man's vital signs as best he could without proper equipment. Around him, the plane continued to shake and drop altitude. Every instinct told Marcus they were in serious danger, but he forced himself to focus on what he could control. He could help this man. He could keep him stable until they landed. The flight attendants moved through the cabin with remarkable composure, checking seat belts, securing loose items, preparing passengers for the emergency landing. Marcus caught the eye of one attendant, a young man with red hair, and gave him a thumbs up to indicate his patient was stable for now. The attendant nodded gratefully before moving on to the next row. The 20 minutes felt like hours. The plane descended rapidly, the pressure change making Marcus's ears pop painfully. He kept his hand on the elderly man's wrist, monitoring his pulse, offering reassurance. The woman beside Marcus was crying quietly now, her hands clasped tightly together. Through the window, Marcus could see the ground rushing up to meet them. The Indianapolis airport coming into view. The landing was rough but controlled. The plane hit the runway hard, bounced once, twice, then skidded along the tarmac with the screech of brakes and burning rubber. Luggage compartments flew open, spilling bags into the aisles. A woman's laptop flew past Marcus's head, missing him by inches. The plane tilted dangerously to one side before finally, mercifully coming to a stop. For a moment, there was absolute silence. Then the cabin erupted in cheers and applause. People were crying, laughing, hugging strangers, thanking the pilots and crew. Marcus exhaled deeply, his shoulders sagging with relief. They had made it. They were alive. He checked on the elderly man, who was weak but responsive. The woman beside Marcus grabbed his hand and squeezed it, tears streaming down her face. Emergency vehicles surrounded the plane. their lights flashing through the windows. Clear and authoritative, she thanked everyone for their cooperation during the emergency. She praised the passengers for remaining calm. And then she said something that made Marcus's blood run cold. Marcus felt every eye in the cabin searching, scanning faces, looking for the man whose name had just been called. His hand was still being held by the woman beside him, and he felt her grip loosen, felt her pull away slightly. The elderly man across the aisle stared at him with confusion and something else, something that looked like suspicion. Marcus raised his hands slowly, his voice steady despite the anxiety rising in his chest. He identified himself as Marcus Williams, seat 12A. He was a doctor, he added, unsure why he felt the need to explain. The head flight attendant made her way down the aisle toward him, her expression unreadable. She was a tall woman in her 50s with short gray hair and sharp eyes. She stopped beside his row and asked Marcus to come with her to the front of the plane. She did not smile. She did not explain. Marcus unfastened his seat belt and stood, aware of the dozens of faces watching him. The cabin that had been full of celebration moments ago now felt tense and hostile. He could hear whispers starting, could feel the shift in energy. What had he done? Why were they singling him out? He followed the attendant up the aisle, his mind racing through possibilities. Had someone accused him of something? Had his help with the elderly man been seen as inappropriate somehow? Nothing made sense. The attendant led him past the first class cabin to the galley area near the cockpit. Two other flight attendants stood there along with a man in a pilot's uniform. They all looked at Marcus with expressions he could not quite read. Professional, guarded. The head attendant spoke first. She asked Marcus if he had interfered with any passenger during the flight. Marcus's confusion deepened. He explained about the elderly man, about providing medical assistance, about helping the woman next to him with her oxygen mask. He had identified himself as a doctor. He had only tried to help. The pilot cut him off. They had received a complaint. A passenger had reported that Marcus had been acting suspiciously during the emergency, that he had been touching other passengers without permission, that his behavior had made people uncomfortable. Marcus felt the floor drop out from under him. The accusation was so absurd, so disconnected from reality that for a moment he could not even form words to respond. He had been helping people. He had potentially saved a man's life and now he was being accused of what exactly? Suspicious behavior. The attendant's voice was firm but not unkind. She explained that they had to take all complaints seriously, especially given the circumstances of the emergency landing. They needed to ask him some questions before allowing passengers to disembark. Marcus felt anger rising in his chest, hot and sharp. He asked who had made the complaint. What specifically was he being accused of? The crew members exchanged glances. They could not share that information, they said, but several passengers had expressed concern about his actions during the flight. Several passengers. Marcus thought about the cheers that had filled the cabin just minutes ago. He thought about the gratitude in the elderly man's eyes, the woman's hand squeezing his in relief. And now, suddenly, he was a threat. He was suspicious. He was someone who needed to be questioned and detained while everyone else walked free. Marcus took a deep breath and forced himself to stay calm. He explained his credentials slowly. He was a cardiothoracic surgeon at Emory University Hospital. He had been attending a medical conference in Chicago. He had provided emergency medical care to a passenger who appeared to be experiencing cardiac distress. He had done exactly what any doctor would do, what he was ethically obligated to do. He could provide identification, medical credentials, contact information for the hospital, anything they needed to verify his story. The pilot's expression softened slightly, but the attendants remained guarded. They asked for his identification. Marcus reached for his wallet, moving slowly and deliberately, hyper aware that his every movement was being scrutinized. He handed over his driver's license and his hospital identification badge with his photo and credentials clearly displayed. The head attendant examined the ID cards carefully, then excused herself to make a phone call. Marcus stood in the cramped galley space, flanked by crew members, feeling like a criminal. Through the gap in the curtain, he could see passengers in their seats, some craning their necks to see what was happening, others pointedly looking away. He wondered what they were thinking, what stories were being constructed about him in their minds. The attendant returned after what felt like an eternity. She handed Marcus his identification and said they had verified his credentials with the hospital. They appreciated his assistance during the emergency. However, they still needed to file a report about the complaint. Airport security would need to speak with him before he could leave the terminal. Marcus felt exhaustion wash over him. Even with his credentials verified, even with his innocence clear, the process would continue. The suspicion had been planted and now it had to be investigated, documented, filed away in some database. His name would be associated with this incident forever. A complaint made during an emergency landing. Suspicious behavior. It did not matter that he had been helping. It did not matter that he had potentially saved a life. The passengers began to disembark. Marcus was told to wait until everyone else had left the plane. He stood in the galley, watching the stream of people filing past. Some glanced at him with curiosity. Others avoided looking at him altogether. The elderly man he had helped paused as he passed, supported by a flight attendant. He met Marcus's eyes and nodded, a small gesture of thanks or acknowledgement. Marcus nodded back, grateful for that small moment of recognition. The woman who had been sitting beside him walked past without looking at him, her eyes fixed straight ahead. The plane emptied slowly. Families with small children. Business travelers with their carry-on bags, college students with their backpacks. All of them free to go to continue their journeys to tell their stories about the emergency landing and how they survived. Marcus remained behind waiting. When the last passenger had deplaned, two airport security officers boarded. They were professional but firm. They asked Marcus to accompany them to a private office in the terminal. They assured him this was just standard procedure. Nothing to worry about, just a few questions. Marcus followed them off the plane and through the terminal. People stared as they passed. A black man being escorted by security officers. Marcus kept his head up, his expression neutral, but inside he felt humiliated and angry. In the security office, the questions began again. Could he explain his actions during the flight? Why had he been touching other passengers? Had anyone given him permission to provide medical care? Marcus answered each question with as much patience as he could muster. He explained again about his medical training, his ethical obligation to help, the nature of the emergency. He provided additional documentation, phone numbers for colleagues who could vouch for him, a detailed account of exactly what he had done and why. The officers typed his answers into their computers, nodding occasionally, their faces betraying nothing. After an hour of questioning, they finally told him he was free to go. There would be no charges. The complaint had been investigated and found to be without merit. They apologized for the inconvenience and thanked him for his cooperation. Marcus left the office feeling drained and hollow. He collected his luggage and made his way through the terminal, past the restaurants and shops, past the other travelers going about their normal days. He called his wife from the gate while waiting for the next flight to Atlanta. His voice cracked as he told her what had happened. She listened in silence and when he finished, she simply said she was sorry. She said she loved him. She said she was glad he was safe. She did not say she was surprised because they both knew this was not the first time something like this had happened would not be the last. Marcus had been followed in stores, watched by security guards, pulled over by police for no reason. He had been questioned about whether he really was a doctor, whether he really belonged in the upscale neighborhood where he lived, whether he really was the parent of his light-skinned daughter. He had learned to carry extra identification, to speak carefully, to make himself as non-threatening as possible. But even that was not always enough. On the plane, helping people during an emergency, he had still been seen as a threat. The flight to Atlanta was uneventful. Marcus sat in silence, too tired to work, too wound up to sleep. He thought about the elderly man whose life he might have saved. He thought about the passengers who had cheered their survival one moment and suspected him the next. He thought about his daughter's birthday party, about the normal life he would return to, about the weight he carried the others did not see or understand. When the plane landed in Atlanta, there was no applause, just the usual shuffle of passengers, gathering their belongings, and heading for the exits. Marcus waited until most people had deplaned before standing. He walked through the familiar airport, through the crowds of travelers, out into the warm Georgia evening. His wife was waiting for him at arrivals, and when he saw her, something inside him finally broke. She held him while he cried right there in the middle of the airport. Not caring who saw Dot. The next week, Marcus went back to work. He performed surgeries, saved lives, taught residents, attended meetings. He helped plan his daughter's birthday party. He smiled and laughed and went through the motions of his normal life. But something had shifted. The incident on the plane had reminded him of a truth he sometimes tried to forget. that no amount of education, no level of professional success, no credentials or accomplishments could fully protect him from suspicion. That in moments of crisis when people were scared and looking for someone to blame, he would always be an easy target. But Marcus also refused to let that truth define him. He had helped someone who needed help. He had done what was right and he would continue to do so regardless of the cost because that was who he was. Not the suspicious stranger some passengers had seen. Not the threat that had to be investigated and contained, but a healer, a father, a man who had dedicated his life to saving others. Is there a medical professional on board? I don't know why I'm holding a mic like this. They have phones. The flight attendant's right next to me. I'm a doctor, I can help. I'm not wearing shoes. I have a hoodie on. And I look like I haven't slept in days because my strategy was to sleep on the plane going there to reset my circadian rhythm. The flight attendant does believe me that I'm a physician, brings me over, and I meet the passenger. He's a young guy, and he tells me that he's starting to have some swelling in his hands. And when I look at it, I see hives are present. So, he's having an allergic reaction. And he said he's had an allergic reaction the night prior, but it wasn't that bad. He took some Benadryl and it went away. Now, in that moment, I asked him if he had the allergy medication still on him, and he did. He had some steroid on him, Prednisone, and Benadryl. I instructed him to take another dose of both and to let me know if anything at all starts happening in his mouth or throat, because that could be a sign of anaphylaxis, which means that you have swelling of the upper airways, which essentially, if left untreated, can lead to death, and very, very quickly. Now, you might be wondering, what is giving Matt this serious anaphylactic reaction? He didn't eat on the plane. My suspicion, based on talking with him and hearing his history, he was eating a significant amount of red meat the night prior. Why is this interesting? He normally doesn't eat red meat. He also says not too long ago he was bitten by a tick. Now, if you're bitten by a specific tick called the lone star tick, which exists in the southernmost region of the United States but now is slowly creeping its way up north, you can develop an allergy to red meat, specifically to alpha-gal. This is a sugar molecule that's found in some mammals, but not people. And once you're bitten by this tick, if you eat an animal product that has alpha-gal in it, you can have a delayed allergic reaction much like Matt had. I right away started thinking worst-case scenarios. What's gonna happen if his throat starts swelling and we're over the Atlantic Ocean? Well, we can't land, so I would have to administer epinephrine, which is essentially just adrenaline. And we give this medicine into the upper thigh, into the muscle, in order to relax the airways, to tense up the blood vessels throughout the body so that we don't lose blood pressure, which is one of the really dangerous parts of anaphylactic shock. If the epinephrine or the EpiPen does not work, the next step would be to schedule a landing. And if the swelling continues to get worse, I would be forced to make an incision in this young man's throat. I started panicking. I haven't done this procedure since medical school. I haven't seen it performed in years. I tried connecting to the wifi to watch videos. I have no shame in saying this because this is not a procedure we do anymore. We intubate patients. But to do what we call a cric, that's serious stuff. That's what you see on Grey's Anatomy and House episodes that we laugh at and say, oh, no one ever does this. So, I was trying to brainstorm what I can use in terms of tools on the airplane to make this incision. I was saying maybe a pen, maybe a needle, maybe one of the ampules that were found in the kit that were made of glass, and I could use a sharp piece of glass. My mind was racing. A few minutes later, the flight attendant comes and gets me and says, "Okay, he's starting to have oral symptoms." And that's when I kick into my medical mode, full-on medical mode. I rush over, I look at his throat. It is definitely swelling. I can see it swelling. I just make the decision to rush him to the front of the plane, right by the cockpit, and it allows us to get some privacy, 'cause I was gonna ask him to take off his pants in order to give him the injection. When I break open the kit that was found on Delta's plane, I was shocked because on the outside of the kit, it said epinephrine. There were no EpiPens. Now, you might be thinking, well, if there's epinephrine, what's the big deal? You have epinephrine. It can be used just like an EpiPen. Well, yes and no. While it's the same active ingredient, the dosages are different. Normally you give either .3 milligrams or .5 milligrams during an anaphylactic reaction. With an EpiPen, that's already pre-administered and pre-filled with the needle in it and everything. Now, what was in Delta's kit was an epinephrine injection kit for cardiac arrest. That's when someone's heart flat-lines. You're not shocking a patient. You're giving them this dose of epinephrine. This is much higher dose. It's one milligram. And the needle is an inch and a half long. And I knew this was gonna be painful. So, here I am reading the instructions on how to get the needle free, because this isn't a kit I was familiar with using. So, I was fumbling with it for the first 30 seconds or so. That's why we need to have an EpiPen on board. Because an EpiPen, had it been there, all you have to do is read the instructions really quickly, boom, it auto-injects. It's simple. I told the patient what we're gonna do, I guess the passenger, told the flight attendant, and I injected it because his throat was really starting to swell. He started screaming. I immediately withdraw the needle. I hand him an alcohol pad and I ask him to hold pressure so I didn't get any of his blood on me. He starts telling me that the area's becoming very tender, and I understand why. One of the main mechanisms of action of how epinephrine works is vasal constriction. It makes the arteries clamp down. That raises the blood pressure and allows the person to live. But also, if you're giving it in this area and it tightens all the blood vessels up in this area, it causes a lot of pain. So, right away I explained to him what was going on. I had him take a few steps to walk around, got some ice on his leg just to make the pain a little bit less. We're checking his pulses, checking his vitals. The reason why when you administer epinephrine or an EpiPen the first step we do afterwards is to get you to a hospital is for monitoring, to make sure that, A, the allergic reaction doesn't come back, B, to make sure that your heart is okay because I'm giving you adrenaline. That not only speeds up the heart, raises the blood pressure. It can cause all sorts of side effects. So, now above the Atlantic Ocean, the pilots are asking me, the flight attendants are asking me, should we land in Canada? Should we go back? Should we land on the islands near Portugal? I ask the head flight attendant, who was super sweet, to ask ground control what their policy was. And what she told me Delta told her, as long as a doctor was in control of the situation and felt the patient was stable, no emergency diversion was needed. So, I checked his vitals a few times after doing the injection. He was tachycardic as a result of the epinephrine. His blood pressure was stable. It was in the normal range. It wasn't dropping. And we made the decision to continue on route to Tel Aviv, Israel, but continually monitoring his status. I took out my iPhone. I was looking in his throat just to make sure it wasn't swelling. Because he did scare me a couple of times. He couldn't tell if it was just very dry or starting to swell. Whenever you get a dose of adrenaline, I guess think about if you're making a speech and you're doing public speaking. You get nervous, you get dry mouth. That's the adrenaline kicking in there. So I thought that that's what likely was going on because when I looked with my iPhone, I did not see any swelling. Everything looked okay. There was no need to give a second injection. So, what my job was once we gave the injection was to, A, keep the patient calm, and to check his vitals to make sure there's nothing bad changing, his blood pressure wasn't dropping, his pulse wasn't going too high, and we could continue on with our flight. I did this pretty much every 30 minutes, talked to him, stood over him. And at one point, he became really tired because he took several doses of Benadryl, Prednisone, adrenaline. He was getting sleepy 'cause he didn't sleep well the night before. So, after watching him for about four or five hours after the injection, I felt comfortable returning him to his seat, telling his counselors and people that he was on the trip with about what was going on, that if anything were to change to right away come and get me. But my plan was to put him in his seat for the last two hours and every half hour or so come on and check on him and do another round of vitals. I did that up until the point of landing. His vitals were great. He was resting, his throat wasn't swelling, his pain in his leg was even resolving. So, we're able to land the plane and he survived this transatlantic flight. The passengers around me thanked me, but they didn't thank me for saving this young man's life. I guess they didn't know what was going on. They thanked me for not diverting the plane and ruining their plans. I thought that was pretty funny. The crew on the plane were amazing. All the pilots, flight attendants, they actually wrote me a nice thank-you card for the attention that I gave Matt. I was nervous, I was sleep-deprived, but I was also very happy that we had a good result. What makes this story kind of come full circle is really interesting. There's a company called Taglit, which does birthright trips to Israel. And that's the trip that Matt was on. He was going to explore Israel with his group. I actually was on the same trip 10 years prior. And they said, well, we have to connect them somewhere where we meet on this trip. He was gonna be in Jerusalem. I was gonna be in Jerusalem. We had to have a meetup. And what do you know? We made it happen. I was in a hospital in Jerusalem doing some talks with one of their university researchers and touring their hospital, and we found Matt there. He was already discharged, but he came to visit. We're able to say hi, get a good laugh. I was able to see that he's perfectly fine. His leg is not bothering him too much, he's touring all around Israel doing hikes no problem. Now he's carrying an EpiPen around to not repeat this experience. How is it possible that airlines don't have EpiPens on board? Allergies, anaphylactic attacks are on the rise all throughout the world. We should be having EpiPens available so that the flight attendants could be trained on how to use them, and they can hopefully save someone's life. I looked up if this has ever happened before, and it has. Luckily in those situations, another passenger had an EpiPen on board, and they were able to use theirs. But relying on that is not smart. Relying on the fact that there's epinephrine for cardiac arrest on the plane is also not smart. Easily in the haste of an emergency, someone's gonna take that, administer way too much of a dose, and the person can die as a result. My message to airlines? Do what you have to do to get an epinephrine pen, specifically an auto-injector pen, on board, update it every year or 18 months so it doesn't expire, and you're gonna save people's lives. I think this is just a no-brainer that we absolutely have to accomplish. This story was obviously a crazy one. I don't know if I'm ever gonna have a story time to top this one. If you wanna hear about my journey to Israel and all the amazing things I was able to accomplish there, drop it down below in the comments, 'cause I desperately wanna tell you about some of the amazing breakthroughs they having going on there. And definitely click this video 'cause it's worth watching, and I know you're gonna love it. As always, stay happy and healthy. - Thank you, Dr. Mike. I really appreciate your help, man. Meet RFDS Pilot Dave Rogers | The Flying Doctor Podcast: Kind: captions Language: en We interview people from across Australia in the footprint of more than 250 indigenous nations. Put the stretcher back in. Shut the door. Off we go again. This is the Flying Doctor podcast and I'm your host Lana Howorth. Each week we fly to rural and remote communities across Australia and hear the stories of real life patients who've needed a call on the Royal Flying Doctor Service. Yeah. So, I'm Dave Rogers and I'm the senior base pilot out at the Broken Hill base of the Royal Flying Doctor Service Southeastern section. So, my role as well as doing all the same duties as our regular line pilots, I also am the supervisor of our team of pilots. I mean, there's a few extra bits and pieces that I get up to around the base and I'm sort of the contact point for the other departments as well. If they have any questions aviation wise and and of my team, they sort of come through me. All right. Well, let's go back to baby Dave and growing up. Where did you grow up and when did you first know that you wanted to become a pilot? Yeah. Yeah. Look, I grew up in Melbourne, which, you know, people from up here call me a bit of a Mexican sometimes and tease me about it, but look, I haven't lived in Melbourne for many years. Um, and look, as a kid, I'd always had an interest, I guess, in aviation. I probably, you know, some pilots say that, you know, as soon as they could take steps, they looked up at the sky and saw a plane and then they decided at that point that they wanted to be a pilot. I wouldn't say that was the case for me. As I said, I'd always had an interest, but probably didn't really know what I wanted to do through most of high school. And it was getting to sort of year 9 and 10 I think where you know your teachers were starting to encourage you to really think about where you wanted to go in life career-wise so you could tailor I guess your schooling towards that. Yeah. So as part of that process one of my teachers you know just suggested you know maybe aviation might be something I'd be interested in. And so I did a a trial flight sort of at that time and then really fell in love with it. And so yeah, from I guess late high school, that was probably the first point in my life where I said, "No, this is something I really want to do. This is a lot of fun." And so, yeah, I sort of went straight into it from there. For those mere mortals that haven't flown a plane or even been in a small aircraft, can you describe what a lot of people they say is an addiction once you once you start to fly? Yeah, look, yeah, it's probably it's probably an accurate description really. It is an addiction and and the longer you spend away from it. We were just talking before we jumped onto this that I've had a broken arm. So I've been away from flying for 6 weeks and you really start to miss it the longer you're away. So yeah. Yeah. Certainly when you when you have a taste of it for a lot of us it just it does become something you just want to keep doing. I I remember quite vividly the first flight I ever took in a light aircraft had my breath taken away by just the scenery, the different perspective. and you know we take it a bit for granted I guess being seeing it every day but it is it's truly a privilege to to work in the area that I do. It's it's a fantastic job. So when did you decide that once you got your wings once you knew this was what you wanted to do for a living that then you wanted to go to the RFDS and how did that all happen? A bit of backstory to that. I mean I got my commercial license in 2010. So I I did all my training in Victoria. I did a uni degree in aviation at the same time. And I mean any pilot would know sometimes finding that first job is the most difficult one. And you know there's there's people that you know will go remote and go north to Darwin or or you know far west sort of Queensland and and find sort of a station job or a charter flying job. I wanted to go down the instructing route which is another way to sort of get an entry- level job and and build experience. So I was privileged to get a job in country Victoria as a fly instructor. very junior as I said only had just got my license but they were really good to me and invested a lot of you know time and money in terms of me developing my skills so I started from I guess doing very basic initial training and then over the course of three or four years in that role you know started out in qualifications things like doing multi-engine training instrument training night training and then eventually I was also training instructors so training other people to teach students to fly and then I guess from there so I' I'd done that job for 4 years and and decided I needed a bit more diverse experience cuz you know in the training environment I guess you're limited in terms of some of the scenarios you'll expose your students too. So you know I didn't fly in a lot of bad weather and you know you'd go to generally pretty well-maintained airports just by nature of the environment of training and so I decided I needed to look further a field to broaden I guess my skill set. And so that that led me to to make an interesting move over to PNG up in New Guinea. And I spent the next three years, so this is from about 2015 to 2018, flying up in the highlands of PNG. And I was flying a single engine 8-seater aircraft. I mean, going into a lot of bush strips, a lot of these villages only had an airirstrip in terms of their access to the outside world. And that was a really stretching time, I guess, in terms of my professional skills. you know, exposed me obviously to a lot of remote area flying, but weather challenges in terms of the infrastructure over there aviation wise, it's there's really nothing compared to what we have here. Even in in remote areas of New South Wales where I'm working now, it was pretty pretty rugged and you were kind of more or less on your own in a lot of ways. You didn't have a lot of support in air traffic control instrument approaches. So, yeah, I really sort of I guess honed my skills having to work in that environment. So after that, I had a young family with me at the time and we decided we're ready back to come back to Australia. And so in 2019, we came back and we had our our fourth child when we got back into the country. And I picked up a job doing some more instructing just as an easy way to get back into things in Australia. And then, you know, I was thinking maybe I should go to an airline job. And then I'd done some interviews for that. And then CO hit, which was obviously an interesting time for everyone, particularly for those in the aviation industry. So when that happened, it became clear that the industry was going to grind to a halt, particularly airlines in terms of hiring. And I'd seen this job ad for the RFDS and I yeah, I put put my hat in the ring and to be honest, I didn't have high expectations of my chances given how many other pilots were around the place looking for work. But yeah, obviously obviously it went well given I'm I'm here 5 years later still working the job. And I'm so glad to be honest that I didn't go down the airline path. I think this is this is the best job you can get in aviation in terms of all that it brings and the variety. So that's a very long answer I guess Sophie in terms of how I ended up in the RFDS but it's hard to give a short answer I suppose but it's an answer that brings so many questions. The first one is how does your family and your you know family network cope with you doing this and particularly even that time in PNG. I mean there must have been a lot of worrying moments where they were like Dave Dave are you are you home yet? Where are you? Yeah. Yeah. No, I think it was very hard for my mom. In fact, the whole aviation journey has been stressful. I remember early days, even when I was training, she'd see a headline about some sort of aviation accident. She'd call me to make sure I was okay, even though I was nowhere near me. So, certainly there's risks to the job and, you know, PNG, as as I kind of touched on brought, you know, some interesting risks and unique risks and challenges flying over there. you kind of I mean you get used to it and I can't really I suppose speak on behalf of my wife but she's she's a pretty relaxed person which I think you probably have to be partnered up with someone like me. So yeah, she's she's found ways to manage and cope with that over the journey. And I think too when we talk about particularly RFDS pilots and and you know things like the recent TV series and lots of stories around the RFDS give the general public an impression that you know really you guys are the best of the best as far as gun pilots. But those extra challenges, they must add lots of dimensions and layers to what you do every day. You describe the air strips. I mean, paint us a bit of a picture about some of the air strips that you've had to to land on. So I'm kind of used to I guess you know different experiences I suppose but I mean the unique challenges we have here is you know again you're a long way from things like air traffic control. We don't have instrument approaches. You know some of our strips have lighting but in terms of the guidance to get us down to land you're on your own there. That's where you really have to draw on, I guess, your own experience and capacity as a pilot to be able to still safely operate without those extra supports that you might get in some bigger locations. But yeah, a lot of the people on property, you know, they're often doing flying themselves. And so yeah, most most of the people that we work with are pretty good in in keeping on top of their airirst strips and and looking after us in that regard because obviously, you know, they they want us to be able to be there, you know, in their time of need and and we want to be able to get to them. So yeah. No, but and we're trained very well to be able to deal with that. You know, we do quite an extensive period of induction when you when you join the RFDS. Yeah. Yeah. Absolutely. Yeah. No, and look, in my experience, a lot of these, you know, incidents and accidents do happen at the worst times of the day, late in the day or in the middle of the night. And I'd done all my figures and calculations and I'd factored in, you know, a certain amount of, you know, payload reduction based on how I felt the surface was. And so as a result of that, you'd often take off and land in only one direction. So you you'd land uphill and then take off downhill. And what what that meant given the short nature and the steepness of some of the strips basically a lot of the time once you started your takeoff you were you were going one way or another. And then literally the first thing they told me was that there was an explosion up at a pub in Tibborara. And so, you know, my mind starts racing from there, thinking, gee, wow, okay, you know, that it's bad. And and that was still in the very early stages, I think, of of working out, you know, how many injured there might be, what exactly, you know, we were going to face when we got there. But I just knew, I guess, when that call came in that I was going to have to move quickly. And so, I jumped back in my my work clothes and drove out to the airport. And as as we sort of were getting ready, more details were coming in. And you know, typically when we respond to a retrieval, it's it's usual you only have one patient. Sometimes you'll have multiple if it's things like a motor vehicle accident. But what what had happened was we we got told that there was certainly three potentially more trauma patients that we were going to have to to manage, which we can only take two on our planes. And so we knew right at that point that this was going to need probably more sets of hands than we typically would take. So beyond our normal complement of pilot, nurse and doctor, we made the call to bring in the dayshift pilot who were just sort of signing off from work and the dayshift nurse and dayshift doctor to come along for the ride as well. And adding to so I'm I'm sort of crunching all these numbers and this is you know to be to be honest a little bit stressful being the first one where I'm on my own and I'm dealing with this very complex scenario that I hadn't encountered yet. I'd been to Tibber, but I think I'd only been there during the day. Hadn't been there at night. And here I am sort of trying to manage this situation all on my own. And then to to make it more complicated, got a call from the doctor saying that they'd had the fire brigade on the phone and they were wondering whether they could jump on board to head up because three of the injured were actually people that were fighting the fire. So, I mean, to cut a long story short, I guess. So, we ended up jumping in into the plane. We had my nurse, our doctor. We also had the day nurse, the day doctor. And we got up there and if you know Timber, the health service up there, there are some amazing people up there that manage that facility. Basically, we got up there and and the ladies up there had pretty much turned it into a trauma center. Literally, we were taking off, I think, after working all night just on sunrise out of Tibber. - How Do Pilots Pass The Time On Long Flights?: Kind: captions Language: en For most passengers, long-haul flights are&nbsp; a matter of endurance aided by a few drinks,&nbsp;&nbsp; inflight entertainment, and trying to get some&nbsp; sleep between meal services. It is an entirely&nbsp;&nbsp; different matter for the people flying the plane&nbsp; as there are still plenty of things to do even&nbsp;&nbsp; after takeoff. Let’s look at what’s happening&nbsp; on the flight deck during these longer flights… Even though flight paths are decided upon&nbsp; before departure, pilots have to monitor&nbsp;&nbsp; weather conditions and decide whether&nbsp; or not to alter them during the flight.&nbsp;&nbsp; On long-haul flights, weather conditions are&nbsp; more likely to change from initial forecasts,&nbsp;&nbsp; with aircraft often encountering multiple&nbsp; weather systems. These systems can vary in&nbsp;&nbsp; intensity from slight turbulence to dangerous&nbsp; thunderstorms. Traveling at high speeds,&nbsp;&nbsp; pilots must be prepared at all times&nbsp; to alter the flight path if necessary. If, for example, they see a&nbsp; thunderstorm on the radar,&nbsp;&nbsp; they must first get the OK from air traffic&nbsp; control (ATC) to change their route. Clear air turbulence is another worry, and&nbsp; because it cannot be seen on the radar,&nbsp;&nbsp; pilots rely upon the information provided by&nbsp; pilots flying the same route ahead of them. When&nbsp;&nbsp; flying across oceans such as the Atlantic, one&nbsp; pilot's job is to communicate with the ATC while&nbsp;&nbsp; the other monitors a common air-to-air frequency&nbsp; that pilots use to communicate with each other. The entire time a flight is&nbsp; in the air, the pilots monitor&nbsp;&nbsp; all kinds of gauges to ensure that the&nbsp; plane performs how it is supposed to. Making sure that the temperature of the fuel&nbsp; is not too cold is another thing pilots must&nbsp;&nbsp; monitor. If the fuel cools down too much,&nbsp; its flow can be restricted due to waxing.&nbsp;&nbsp; If this happens, pilots can rectify&nbsp; the situation by descending, or by&nbsp;&nbsp; increasing the aircraft's speed (and,&nbsp; thus, its skin temperature). However,&nbsp;&nbsp; this isn't a particularly frequent occurrence,&nbsp; as engine oil helps fuel to retain heat. While one of the pilots monitors all the&nbsp; gauges, the other will be busy taking care&nbsp;&nbsp; of the paperwork. Before the flight, the&nbsp; pilots are given a written flight plan.&nbsp;&nbsp; Anything that deviates from this&nbsp; must be written down as notes. Outside of these important activities,&nbsp; long-haul flying can be very boring for pilots.&nbsp;&nbsp; During cruise phases, pilots will often pass&nbsp; the time by making small talk with one another,&nbsp;&nbsp; or doing some reading. Smartphone games and&nbsp; good old fashioned crossword puzzles might be&nbsp;&nbsp; additional forms of entertainment&nbsp; found on the flight deck. Finally, just like passengers,&nbsp; enjoying inflight meals&nbsp;&nbsp; is another part of the long-haul pilot experience. The pilots typically wait until the passengers&nbsp; have been served and then eat separately.&nbsp;&nbsp; They do this so that there is&nbsp; always one person at the controls. For long flights over twelve hours, there&nbsp; are one or two relief pilots onboard&nbsp;&nbsp; so that the two pilots who performed the&nbsp; takeoff can take a break and get some rest. There are just two pilots on shorter&nbsp; long-haul flights of eight or nine&nbsp;&nbsp; hours. The only opportunity they get to&nbsp; leave their seats and stretch their legs&nbsp;&nbsp; is when they go to the bathroom. Did you know about these long-haul duties and&nbsp; activities? Let us know by leaving a comment.

overnight and if it's a short over night finding a time to go work out in the hotel gym go on a run or find a gym nearby might be really hard I struggled with that at the beginning and I really had to find a way of making that a priority for me on overnights where it was feasible where I wasn't just at the minimum rest requirement for the overnight to actually go to the gym to try to stay in shape on the road the other half of that is that when you're home you only have a few days off right so you have to manage your entire life you have to go grocery shopping catch up on errands and chores and have time to relax and see friends and in between all of that you stop to find time to go to the gym so it's a lot to manage that was a really hard part about starting number seven before I started I wish I had a better idea of the dip between trip efficiency and flight hours between smaller regional jets and larger regional jets so here on the Embraer 145 many of our flights are less than an hour and a half long so for a four-day trip to meet a good threshold of flight time and block time and thus pay you oftentimes have to work many more flights throughout that four-day trip that's a lot more work than you might find on the 175 which has longer routes with less flights throughout the day that's a huge difference when you're talking about the actual amount of work that's going into your job versus just a basic amount of pay or a basic amount of flight hours number eight now something specific to the airlines and jet flying in general is that visual approaches are much more challenging than in slower aircraft in the general aviation world now keep in mind one of the easiest approaches that we have at the airline is flying an ILS sometimes even down the minimums because we have lateral and vertical guidance almost all the way to the runway people start to get into trouble when they mess up visual approaches when they're too fast especially at night or they overshoot finally managing your speed and configuration in de jet is much more difficult on a visual approach than in a smaller aircraft because you just have less time to get ready for landing now that's why we always follow an instrument approach all the way to the runway when we have one available as a backup to make that visual approach a little bit easier number nine the destination for an overnight isn't nearly as important as your attitude or the attitude of the crew that you're flying with in terms of whether or not people are willing to be adventurous to go out and explore a little bit and find group activities I've had some of my best overnights and places like Manhattan Kansas or Dubuque Iowa whereas my overnights where I've been alone and had nothing to do in New York City have been not nearly as fun so try not to focus on whether or not the destination is exotic but whether or not you and your crew is willing to try to find something fun to do as a group number 10 the last thing that I wish I would have known before becoming an area pilot is that when you're a student or CFI the idea of flying up to five legs a day for an airline might sound really exciting but once you do it over and over it is a job it is tiring work and you might feel exhausted by the end of the day especially when you've dealt with weather and maintenance issues and delays and cancelations it piles up and it is hard work now keep in mind it's all about your attitude choices so when you have those five leg days and you are tired it might not be the fun exciting job that we're hoping for but you're still flying an airplane you're still getting paid to be a pilot and it's still a really cool job so trying to keep that attitude positive is one of the things that I wish I would have been better prepared for before starting in an airline that's it those are 10 things that I wish I had known before starting as an airline pilot and I hope it helps you manage your expectations better before you start on your first day at an airline there are plenty of times during this job where you'll be tired because it rarely starts late end times and plenty of days spent away from home but this job is all about having the right attitude choice and that's really the only way that you can make this career the best decision ever

inform the flight crew and carry their medications on them five years ago I was on a flight from New York to LA and there was an 18 year old Korean lady who barely spoke any English and she had her anti-seizure medications in her checked baggage nothing in her carry-on well guess what she had a seizure by the time I was able to get to her the seizure had already stopped but she was post-ictal meaning she had confusion after the seizure once she became more Lucid we were able to figure out that her meds were in her checked baggage and she also didn't know the English name or the medications that she was taking and I was concerned that she would have another seizure this was mid-flight so I asked the flight attendants to make an announcement to ask the passengers on the plane for anti-anxiety medication because anti-anxiety meds also treat and prevent seizures we have the flight attendant ask does anyone have any Klonopin or Xanax and sure enough half the plane puts their hand up and they say they do so I go and speak to someone who's willing to donate some of their Klonopin and be giving some klonopin to our young lady who had the seizure and she didn't have any more seizures for the rest of the flight if a seizure occurs during a flight the surrounding passengers should clear the area and protect the person from injury by cushioning their head and removing any nearby objects now once the seizure ends that person should be placed in the recovery position in medical assistance being sought immediately breathing difficulties can occur for many reasons including pre-existing lung conditions like COPD in some cases the reduced air pressure and humidity in the cabin can exacerbate some respiratory issues passengers with known respiratory conditions should carry their medications and inform the flight crew of their condition if someone experiences breathing difficulties during a flight they should be provided with supplemental oxygen sometimes sudden shortness of breath occurs as a result of a blood clot go into the lungs known as a pulmonary embolism a pulmonary embolism almost always starts out as a clot in the legs meaning a deep vein thrombosis so this blood clot forms in a deep vein usually within the leg and is more likely to form when you're sitting still for too long so long periods of immobility during air travel increases the risk of deep vein thrombosis DVT and hence increases the risk of pulmonary embolism to minimize this risk passengers you're going to want to stay hydrated and perform in seat exercises with your legs and try to take a walk to the restroom this way you promote blood circulation in your legs now compression stockings may also be beneficial a pulmonary embolism depending on how big it is can cause varying degrees of symptoms the symptoms can be variable usually the symptoms it's going to be shortness of breath sometimes you have chest pain but sometimes it can lead to dizziness and it can also cause people to syncope to have loss of consciousness chest pain can be a sign of a serious cardiac event such as a heart attack or it can result from an aortic dissection but it can be also due to non-cardiac causes like indigestion or even a muscle strain regardless of the cause chest pain should never be ignored during a flight I remember another flight that was on in which they asked for a doctor on the plane this man in the 60s was having sudden onset of chest pain he was also very sweaty and I was pretty sure he was having a heart attack thankfully this happened before we took off and the paramedics were able to get him off the plane pretty quickly allergic reactions can range from mild skin irritation to life-threatening anaphylaxis passengers with known allergies should carry appropriate medications including an epinephrine auto-injector and inform the flight crew of their condition in the case of a severe allergic reaction giving someone epinephrine quickly is crucial flight attendants are trained to recognize the signs of anaphylaxis and may carry emergency medications like epinephrine on board gastrointestinal problems talking like nausea vomiting diarrhea are pretty common during air travel the only time that I did not wear a seat belt when the plane was Landing was because they called for a doctor to see this young woman who was vomiting and feeling like she was going to pass out well maybe I think it was a combination of motion sickness from the plane landing but also she was very dehydrated and extremely hungover from the night before the plane landed and she's able to walk off without any difficulty she was fine diabetic emergencies so diabetes it can cause either high blood sugar or low blood sugar when you have high blood sugar that can lead to things like diabetic ketoacy Doses dka and that is a medical emergency but much much more common is when the blood sugar is too low hypoglycemia and that can result in all sorts of bad things the brain starts to function abnormally and that can actually lead to coma and in severe cases death so low blood sugar is not to be messed with so this lady that I saw they called for Doctor of the plane she was a lady with uh metastatic breast cancer and she did have diabetes and she was feeling confused she eventually became minimally responsive we were able to figure out that her blood sugar was low we did check it at some point and it was low we got her some orange juice and some crackers and that improved her condition pretty quickly and she pretty much snapped out of it not too long after that what about obstetric emergencies thankfully I had never had to be called for a pregnant passenger experience in an emergency on the plane pregnancy complications during air travel can include pre-term labor miscarriage or other obstetric emergencies now although most Airlines have restrictions on air travel during late pregnancy emergencies they can still occur so if a pregnant passenger experiences pain or bleeding or other concerning symptoms that's going to be a very unnerving situation so when they ask for is there a doctor on the plane my recommendation is to see what other doctors and Health Care Professionals are on that plane most of the times that this has happened to me there was another doctor or a medical resident or a medical student or a nurse also on that flight so it's good to have open communication and see what their role is and to work as a team so most is there a doctor on the plane moments turn out to be nothing serious but you never know - Avoid This Mistake! | FAA Medical Certificates Explained: Kind: captions Language: en getting your fa medical certificate is the first step to becoming a pilot but it can be a confusing process so in this video we'll explain the different classes of medical certificates the requirements to obtain a medical certificate what the process actually looks like and the worst mistake new pilots make when trying to get a medical certificate medical certificates make sure that the pilot is medically fit to fly and the fa doesn't want to experience a medical emergency while flying an 80ton metal bird with 200 passenger in the back so which type should you get there are three classes of medical certificates first second and third if you want to fly for fun you will need a third class medical certificate this one is valid for 60 calendar months if you are under 40 and 24 calendar months if you are 40 or older if you want to become a commercial pilot you will need a second class medical certificate this one is valid for 12 calendar months regardless of age lastly if you want to be flying for the airlines you will need to get a First Class Medical certificates that this one is valid for 12 calendar months if you're under 40 and if you're older it will be valid for six calendar months but you actually don't even need a medical certificate to fly you can enjoy some of the Privileges of the third class medical certificate by using a program called basic Med now for some Pilots the requirement of a third class medical is pretty restrictive basic Med has different restriction tailored specifically to these aviators for example Pilots over 40 need to renew their third class medical certificate with an fa approved doctor every 2 years but under basic Med they can visit any doctor every 4 years and continue flying because this isn't like your usual medical certificate flying under basic Med comes with some restrictions see the link below for more information about basic Med now that you know which class you need how do you go about getting one applying for or renewing your medical certificate is actually pretty straightforward you can apply for certification through the fa Med X press this is the on online version of the fa form 85008 this is what you need to apply now after you fill out the form you can book an appointment online with a local Aviation medical examiner or am an AM is a doctor that specializes in aviation they will give you a physical exam and when you pass they will issue your medical certificate now I know medical exam can sound like a pain but over 90% of applicants pass their physical exam you just need to be reasonably healthy now the are a few differences in the requirements for each class starting with vision for distance Vision 2040 or better in each eye for third class and 2020 or better in each eye for first and second class for near Vision 2040 or better in each eye with or without correction and for intermediate Vision 2040 or better in each eye for first and second class there are no intermediate Vision requirements for third class if you wear eyeglasses or contact good news you can still get your medical certificate you just have to correct your vision to what's prescribed by the FAA a lot of people think that you can't fly if you are colorblind that's actually not true you can still become a pilot if you have a color vision deficiency but you will be restricted to daytime flying only to get the Restriction lifted you will have to get a statement of demonstrated ability or soda now this is a document that says that you have passed additional testing to lift the Restriction you can obtain a soda for color vision deficiency by passing an alternate color vision test like the forworth Lantern Test we've linked more information about color blindness and becoming a pilot down here in the description now for the hearing requirement you need to be able to hear an average conversation in a chir room with both ears at 6 feet with the back turn to the examiner there's also an audiometric speech discrimination test this actually measures how well you can understand words at different volume levels for heart and blood pressure requirements you'll go through test to determine if you have no problems with your pulse if your heart is healthy if your blood pressure is in the normal range with a maximum of 155 over 90 and for first class if you are over 35 and applying for the first time you'll need to undergo an electroc cardiogram or ECG when you turn 40 you'll need to take the ECG every year you also need to test for your ears nose and throat the am will examine your upper respiratory system and also make sure that there is no issue with your vestibular system last the requirement includes your mental health The Examiner will ask about your mental health and this includes any history of psychosis bipolar disorder and severe personality disorder now there are some conditions that could exclude you from being able to get a medical certificate these are called disqualifying conditions some examples of these are diabetes or coronary heart disease we've added a link to the full list in the description so here's the biggest mistake new pilots make when getting their medical they want to become airline pilots which means that they need a first class
