- Draw My Life - Coming To America: Kind: captions Language: en - A lot of you may not know this, but I actually was born in Russia. (speaks in a foreign language) - Don't nobody understand the words that are coming outta your mouth, man. - Bee-a-whoop, I'm a young five-year-old boy in Russia, and my parents tell me that in a couple weeks, we're about to move to the United States. I can't even comprehend what that means. When you're five, six, your world is, like, this big. My world was preschool, home, maybe the ice cream store. My parents tell me we're moving to America, gave me no information about why we're doing it, how long we're doing it for. They told me I'm not allowed to tell anybody, because it was a secret. I had no idea why this was. I thought they were playing a game with me, actually. We had only 24 hours of notice to our closest, closest friends to say that we were, in fact, leaving. Later on, I've come to realize that we came to the United States on a refugee status visa, I guess, it would say. That was our governmental status. We came here to have a better life for our family, for my sister, et cetera. I vividly remember the flight. Because it was my first time being on an airplane, I was extremely excited, also didn't speak English. My parents didn't speak English, but my sister did. So she was ordering drinks for everybody, even though all she was saying was Coke, Pepsi. I was like, okay, that's not really, you don't get credit for knowing how to say brand names, and I remember the flight attendant was so kind to me that she gave me like this little Delta pin that I was like, oh my God, in America, they give pins for free. I don't have to pay you for this. I didn't know if it was a trick. As a kid, you're like, new land, new people, don't know what's going on. I'm not gonna trust anybody, but once we landed, I remember my uncle coming to the airport. He gave me a gift of, like, hot wheels cars, and in Russia, we didn't have anything. I'm talking, like, I might have one color picture of me growing up. There was no fancy technology. There was no toys. A good birthday present was markers, and little did I know, markers dry out if you leave them in direct sunlight. I left it inside of a car, and I literally was crying because I had no markers, and that was my birthday present, and our cars, by the way, were terrible in Russia. There was a company called Zhiguli. It was like, ugh. If you think whatever car you have now is bad, take a sledge hammer to the rims, to the windows, okay, maybe not that bad, but abuse it for 20 years, and that's our brand new version of our car, and then we took the car ride over to Brooklyn where we were gonna live, and it was during Christmas time. It was early December that we came. So there was lights everywhere, and I was like, oh my God, there's lights. There's holiday spirit. It's so beautiful. Looking back at it now, it was a random neighborhood in Bay Ridge, Brooklyn that I'm looking at now, and I'm like, okay, it just looks fine. Our apartment was only a one bedroom apartment for my dad, my mom, my sister, and me. Our parents' bedroom was the living room, the bedroom was cut in half for me and my sister, and the kitchen served as like the little kitchen table that we ate at with four people, as well as a computer, because we needed a computer for my mom to be able to do some work and for my dad to go to medical school for the second time in his life, in his forties, in a new language. So this apartment was a disaster. The only thing that was great about it, actually, was that it had an enormously long hallway. Me and my dad ended up getting a plastic ball and playing soccer in that hallway kicking the ball back and forth to see who could score, and the more bounces you got across the walls, the easier it was to score, because it was hard to judge where it was gonna go. That was good times. By the way, this housing is like welfare housing, essentially. It was rent-controlled. I'm pretty sure it was $400 a month when we first moved in, maybe even $300 a month, cockroaches galore, mice galore. I was definitely afraid of these mice, because my mom was afraid. So I emulated her fear. You could hear the mice running around at night when we would watch television. So I came to the states, and I didn't go to school right away. We were planning on sending me to school, I think, right after the new year. So I came in the middle of first grade. Going into school when you don't speak the language, teacher's asking you questions right away, right? They want to get to know you. She asks me a question. I say one of the three words I know. I said, "Yes." She bought it. She comes in, asks me another question. I say the second word that I know, "No." She buys it. She asks me another follow-up question. I say, "Maybe." Now she realizes this dude does not speak English. I need to find a translator. Luckily there was two girls in my class, twins, Rita and Rosa, who spoke Russian and English, and they translated. They understood where I could hang my jacket. I was obviously very stressed out coming to a new school, meeting new people. I cried the first day and the second day and the third day. When our parents came to pick us up, my mom came. The teacher grabbed my mom and was telling her, your son is crying a lot. I wanna make sure he's okay, this and that. My mom has no idea what she's saying, because my mom doesn't speak any English yet. Cry, sad, they're using words, sign language, anything they can essentially show to each other that I'm upset, and they want me to feel better. Blah, blah, blah, we figure it out. The one thing I didn't struggle with, even in first grade when I first came over, was math. Because my mom was a math teacher, and in Russia, they're like, you better know everything there is to know about math as a six-year-old, I had the knowledge of a third grader of math. So math was super easy, and I could, sort of, coast on the math component and try really hard on the English and everything else that I was terrible at, and I remember going to ESL and then my grandparents picking me up from school and then telling my grandma like, "I learned a new word today." She's like, "What'd you learn?" I was like, "Hot air balloon." She's like, "What is that?" I'm like, "Oh, it's a thing that flies in the air." My grandpa actually, and my grandma, religiously picked me up from school from grades one to three. After that, they said you're old enough to cut it in New York City. Good luck as a nine-year-old, and we started walking home as a nine-year-old. Luckily I lived one to two blocks away from school. So it was a very short route for me. I had some close friends growing up, some were Russian, some were not. They all lived in the same vicinity, usually in the same building. We played handball a lot. That was the number one sport go to, and then as I started becoming more interested in American culture, I got really into basketball and watching like Michael Jordan and the Bulls play. So I would watch it with my dad, and then my dad took me and got me this, it was like a children's ball, but it had the Bulls logo on it. I treasured it. It was trash, though, like a piece of rubber. It'd bounce too much, and when you threw it against the rim, it bounce like 50 yards back, but then when I went to go play with the regular kids who've been playing basketball their whole lives and they played with the real basketball, I was terrible, because I was getting used to playing with a five inch ball and now I'm playing with a legit-size 29 and a half inch ball. So that was, sort of, my childhood initially coming over as an immigrant. A lot of kids teased you. That was, kind of, the norm when you came from another country. I don't know why it never made me that upset. I think it was because our class was so diverse and so mixed that it seemed like everyone was getting made fun of for something. That just happened to be my thing. Something that also helped, I started doing Taekwondo when I was younger. My parents saved up enough money to literally pay the tuition of the Taekwondo school. That was the max that they could do. They were like, "Okay, you have one after-school activity. "It's Taekwondo. "We want you to do that. "We want you to be in good shape." (people screaming comically) An activity that I had with my dad to do a little father-son bonding when he had time at night was to go onto the street called 86th street in Bay Ridge, Brooklyn that had a lot of stores on it and plan out some of the stuff we hoped to buy when we had some money. One of the stores that we used to go to was called Nobody Beats the Wiz. (upbeat rap music) ♪ Nobody beats the wiz ♪ - and it was an electronics store that sold boom boxes, televisions, they had open box sales, whatever. It was basically like the Best Buy of today, but it wasn't as nice. We were never able to afford anything there, except once, we did buy an open-box boom box, meaning someone bought it, returned it, and it was still open, so we took it, and it was like 200 bucks, and it was the biggest purchase we ever had. Other stores on that journey that we used to go to that I loved, 99 Cent Store, our best friend. I'm talking we bought everything at the 99 Cents Store. Oh, pizza was less than a dollar a slice and Sal's Pizzeria in Brooklyn was the top for me. Brooklyn pizza, nothing tops it. Sorry, Chicago, wherever else people think that there's good pizza. The cheap 75 cents slice that looks like it has oil artificially splurged all over it, delicious. Oh, the number one shoe store that I had all my shoes from, Payless, boom. Love Payless. If it wasn't for Payless, I would never have new shoes. As you could tell, it was a lot of stores where it was humbling, but it's only humbling looking back now, because back then, I thought Payless Shoes was a dope brand. There's so many more options than what we had in Russia. So what you have here and you may think is bad could be way better than what we have in other countries, and I think that's sometimes lost in the messaging of my social media content. People think like, he's successful. That means he was always successful, or he probably comes from a wealthy family. That's furthest from the truth. I had a great family, very supportive family who worked very hard, educated in two countries. There was a lot of work put into that. There was a lot of times where we were on the bottom, bottom, bottom, and in order to get to where we are today, we had to put in a lot of work, and I want you to not lose sight of that, because I think the beauty of where we live here in the United States is that opportunity is available for you. You have to be smart. You have to make some wise choices, and you may mess up, and you may have setbacks, but if you keep working at it, I really think America's the best place for increased opportunities. I mentioned my sister earlier. Well, here's a full video with me and my sister to see how well she knows me. Watch it. It's a blast. She basically roasts me the whole time. As always, stay happy and healthy. I'll see you on this video, though.

Number six: The Piper Saratoga &nbsp; Let's start with the Piper Saratoga, a&nbsp; single-engine powerhouse that's been the&nbsp;&nbsp; gateway drug for countless families entering&nbsp; the world of private aviation. This isn't&nbsp;&nbsp; some glorified Cessna - we're talking about a&nbsp; legitimate cross-country cruiser that can haul&nbsp;&nbsp; six people and their luggage at speeds that&nbsp; make commercial aviation look embarrassing. A trip&nbsp;&nbsp; from New York to Miami that takes most people&nbsp; an entire day becomes a four-and-a-half-hour&nbsp;&nbsp; flight in your own aircraft. Under the hood sits&nbsp;&nbsp; a three-hundred-horsepower Continental&nbsp; engine that propels the Bonanza to cruise&nbsp;&nbsp; speeds of two hundred miles per hour. That's&nbsp; genuinely fast for a single-engine aircraft,&nbsp;&nbsp; and the stability at that speed is remarkable. Number four: The Cirrus SR22 Now let's talk about the Cirrus SR22,&nbsp;&nbsp; the aircraft that revolutionized general aviation&nbsp; with its whole-aircraft parachute system. The SR22&nbsp;&nbsp; is powered by a three-hundred-fifteen-horsepower&nbsp; Continental engine with cruise speeds hitting&nbsp;&nbsp; one hundred eighty-five miles per hour,&nbsp; or over two hundred miles per hour in&nbsp;&nbsp; the turbocharged variant. A new SR22 costs around eight hundred thousand&nbsp; to nine hundred thousand dollars, with used&nbsp;&nbsp; examples starting around four hundred&nbsp; thousand dollars for older models. Number three: The Piper Meridian Moving into turboprop territory, the&nbsp; Piper Meridian represents a massive&nbsp;&nbsp; step up in capability. This single-engine&nbsp; turboprop is powered by a Pratt and Whitney&nbsp;&nbsp; PT6A turboprop engine producing five hundred&nbsp; shaft horsepower. Turboprops start instantly,&nbsp;&nbsp; run smoother than piston engines, and&nbsp; deliver consistent power regardless&nbsp;&nbsp; of altitude. Cruise speeds jump to two hundred&nbsp; sixty miles per hour at thirty thousand feet. The pressurized cabin means no more ear-popping&nbsp; climbs or weather avoidance at low altitudes.&nbsp;&nbsp; Your family travels in shirt-sleeve comfort while&nbsp; the world passes by twenty-five thousand feet&nbsp;&nbsp; below. Range exceeds one thousand miles&nbsp; with full fuel, making coast-to-coast&nbsp;&nbsp; trips possible with just one fuel stop. Expect to invest between one point two million&nbsp;&nbsp; and two point five million dollars for a Meridian.&nbsp; Operating costs jump to around seventy thousand to&nbsp;&nbsp; ninety thousand dollars annually, but you're&nbsp; getting jet-like capability and reliability. Number two: Daher’s TBM series The TBM series from Daher represents&nbsp;&nbsp; the pinnacle of single-engine turboprop&nbsp; performance. These French-built aircraft&nbsp;&nbsp; are essentially jets with propellers,&nbsp; delivering speeds and capabilities&nbsp;&nbsp; that rival light jets. Current TBM&nbsp; models like the nine forty cruise&nbsp;&nbsp; at over three hundred thirty miles per hour at&nbsp; thirty-one thousand feet - faster than many jets.&nbsp;&nbsp; You'll burn about sixty gallons per hour&nbsp; while covering nearly six miles per minute. The high service&nbsp; ceiling and weather radar mean you'll actually&nbsp;&nbsp; complete trips on schedule, regardless of weather&nbsp; conditions that would ground lesser aircraft. Number one: The Pilatus PC twelve Finally, we reach the Holy Grail of&nbsp;&nbsp; family aviation: the Pilatus PC-twelve.&nbsp; This Swiss-built turboprop is powered by&nbsp;&nbsp; a twelve-hundred-shaft-horsepower Pratt and&nbsp; Whitney PT6A engine - more than twice the&nbsp;&nbsp; power of the Meridian. This translates to cruise&nbsp; speeds of two hundred eighty miles per hour and&nbsp;&nbsp; climb rates that put many jets to shame. What makes the PC-twelve special is its&nbsp;&nbsp; versatility. The cabin can be configured as a&nbsp; six-seat luxury transport, an eight-seat commuter,&nbsp;&nbsp; or even a cargo hauler with all seats removed.&nbsp; The flat floor and large cargo door mean you&nbsp;&nbsp; can literally load a motorcycle for&nbsp; backcountry adventures. Range exceeds&nbsp;&nbsp; one thousand eight hundred miles, and&nbsp; it can operate from runways as short&nbsp;&nbsp; as two thousand four hundred feet - opening up&nbsp; thousands of airports that jets cannot access. Operating costs&nbsp; run approximately one hundred fifty&nbsp;&nbsp; thousand dollars annually, but the capability and&nbsp; legendary Swiss reliability justify the investment&nbsp;&nbsp; for families who demand the absolute best. The reality of aircraft ownership extends beyond&nbsp;&nbsp; the purchase price. Hangar costs vary from five&nbsp; hundred dollars monthly in smaller cities to over&nbsp;&nbsp; two thousand dollars in major metropolitan areas.&nbsp; Insurance runs three to five percent of the&nbsp;&nbsp; aircraft's value annually. Annual inspections cost&nbsp; between ten thousand and thirty thousand dollars,&nbsp;&nbsp; while engine overhauls occur every one thousand&nbsp; five hundred to two thousand flight hours, costing&nbsp;&nbsp; fifty thousand to two hundred thousand dollars. However, for families flying more than&nbsp;&nbsp; one hundred hours annually, aircraft ownership&nbsp; often proves more economical than chartering. The&nbsp;&nbsp; break-even point varies, but the convenience&nbsp; and schedule flexibility remain constant&nbsp;&nbsp; regardless of financial considerations. These six aircraft represent realistic&nbsp;&nbsp; options for families serious about private&nbsp; aviation. From the accessible Piper Saratoga&nbsp;&nbsp; to the ultimate PC-twelve, each offers a&nbsp; different balance of performance, capability,&nbsp;&nbsp; and operating costs. The key is matching&nbsp; your mission requirements to the aircraft's&nbsp;&nbsp; capabilities while honestly assessing your budget&nbsp; for both acquisition and ongoing operations.

- The Economics of Private Jets: Kind: captions Language: en This video was made possible by Curiosity Stream. Watch over 2,400 documentaries for free for a month at CuriosityStream.com/Wendover. Private jets represent an inconceivable level of opulence. If the average American were to spend their entire year’s salary to charter a Gulfstream G550 from New York, they would just barely make it to Utah, and yet, there’s a class of people who use these planes to fly not just from New York to Utah, but rather routes like New York to Beijing. There’s a class of people who will spend tens or hundreds of thousands of dollars on just one single flight. Now, there are a few reasons this is strange beyond just the sheer price of things. Chartering that Gulfstream from London to Dubai, for example, you’d end up paying about $55,000 at rack rate. Meanwhile, if you were to fly Emirates First Class, which is just, if not more opulent, you could fly between the cities twenty times for the same price. What’s even stranger about this kind of expense is that businesses, which are intended to maximize profits for their shareholders, are able to justify this enormous expense as worthwhile. So, when is it that paying $8,000 or more an hour to fly makes money? There’s a pretty simple way of figuring this out. Out of the 8,760 hours in a year, the average CEO works 2,716 of them. For a CEO that’s paid $1 million a year, that makes an hour of their time worth $368. Among America’s largest companies, though, the average CEO earns $15.6 million. That makes their hour worth $5,750. For the most part, though, private jets fly about the same speed as commercial planes so when flying a route like London to Dubai, the time savings come at the airports on each end. It comes from being able to arrive, get on a plane, and fly—rather than having to navigate one’s way through a busy terminal for a fixed flight time. But still, flying private versus commercial from London to Dubai would save, at most, about three hours in airport time. With the cost of $55,000 for the flight, that would mean the CEO’s time would have to be worth $18,300 per hour. That wouldn’t be true until they earned $50 million a year—a salary earned only by the upper echelon of CEO’s. But the truth is that, for the most part, private jets do not make economic sense when flying routes with plenty of commercial service like London to Dubai. One of the larger corporate jet fleets out there belongs to Walmart. Now, this might come as a surprise considering that this is a company so focused on keeping costs low. You see, Walmart is headquartered in Bentonville, AR—a relatively small city of 50,000. Their airport does have a surprising amount of service for such a small city with flights all the way to Los Angeles and New York, largely propped up the company’s traffic, but for the higher ups, commercial doesn’t cut it. That’s why the company has a fleet of 20 corporate jets—the largest of any American company. These are most frequently flown by the company’s Regional Vice Presidents who are in charge of a specific area of the country and will have to make frequent store visits within this region. The company apparently has a goal that nobody spends a night away from Bentonville—they want as many trips as possible to be day trips. Now, let’s say that one of these executive vice presidents needs to take a trip to three stores—one in Rock Springs, Wyoming; one in Spokane, Washington; and the last in Great Falls, Montana. Getting to Rock Springs requires a seven hour itinerary through Denver that would get this executive in at 9:30 pm therefore already requiring an overnight stop. Then, the next day, they would do their store visit in the morning and, as there are only two flights a day from Rock Springs, they would have to wait until 4:50 pm to catch a flight back to Denver then another one to Spokane, getting in at 8:30 pm local time, therefore requiring another overnight stop. The next morning they would do their site visit, but once again, flight schedules dictate that the first itinerary to Great Falls would leave at 5:05 pm through Salt Lake City, getting in at 10:04 pm local time, thereby requiring another overnight stop. Following the next morning’s store visit, this executive would catch a noon flight to Denver and, after a three hour layover, another to finally get into Bentonville at 8pm. These three store visits would therefore take up four whole working days, but what if this executive flew private? Leaving at 9am, the first flight direct to Rock Springs would take an hour and 45 minutes getting in, with the hour’s time change, at 9:45 am local time. After a two hour store visit, the plane would take off again at 11:45 am, flying an hour and 15 minutes to Spokane, getting in at noon local time. After another two hour store visit, the plane would take off at 2 pm for a quick 45 minute flight to Great Falls, getting in at 3:45 pm local time. After a final two hours at this store, the plane would take off its final time at 5:45 pm bound for Bentonville. 2 hours and 15 minutes of flight time later, it would land at 9 pm local time, exactly 12 hours after leaving. What was a four day trip on commercial flights becomes a day trip on private, and that’s why Walmart decided private jets are worth it for them. It’s all about valuing the time of their employees and they’ve determined that, even for the relatively low level vice presidents, their time is valuable enough that it’s worth flying them private. For example, one of the aircraft Walmart owns and operates is the Learjet 45. It costs the company about $4 per mile to operate this aircraft including crew, fuel, insurance, maintenance, and all other variable costs. Therefore, the 2,900 miles flown on that day-trip to the north-east would cost them about $11,600. Saving three days, that places a value of $3,900 per day which means that, assuming the executive onboard works every single one of the 260 workdays per year, they would have to make almost exactly $1 million per year for this private jet ride to be worth it to Walmart—an amount within the realm of possibility for upper management at such a large company. Of course, that’s not factoring in the alternative option’s hotel, food, and airline transport costs which would likely sum in the thousands and it’s also assuming there’s just one passenger. If the plane were to be filled to its maximum capacity of nine, each passenger would only need to be paid $111,000 per year for the expense to be worth it to the company which is less than an average Walmart store manager makes. Now, there’s one other case where private jets can make economic sense over flying commercial. Let’s say Walmart was looking to expand into the Philippines. Flying business class, it would cost a minimum of $5,000 roundtrip per person, require three stops, and take over 26 hours to get from Bentonville to Manila. Flying private, though, a long range jet like the Bombardier Global 7500 could make it there non-stop, in just 15 hours, carrying 19 of the company’s top executives. Since the company does not own this type of jet, it would likely charter one at a cost of about $10,000 an hour, or $150,000 for the trip. While the cost of commercial airfare is less than this, assuming the CEO, who makes $24 million a year, is onboard, the value of the eleven hours of his time saved is worth $97,000—clearly tilting the math in favor of the private jet. The general phenomenon of globalization has been great for the private jet market as businesses need to travel to far off places like this. Especially as companies outsource manufacturing and other operations into developing countries, which don't necessary have much air service, many companies have determined that private jets are the best way to get where they need to go. But despite this, the private aviation industry was hit hard but the global financial crisis and still has not fully recovered. While part of it was genuine cost cutting, businesses also wanted to show that they were doubling down on luxuries by getting rid of their jets, even if they could make economic sense in some cases. It was all about optics and nowadays, these jets are coming with poorer and poorer optics, for good reason. Private jets are truly horrendous for the environment. If one were to fly that Bombardier Global 7500, the one that could make it from Bentonville to Manila, with just one passenger onboard, the jet would make it only to South Dakota before that passenger’s carbon footprint exceeded that of the average person in one year. Increasingly, these jets are even being used for purposes that cannot be justified economically. Since 2013, there has been about a 10% increase among Fortune 100 executives of using their company’s corporate jets for personal, leisure purposes. They apparently justify this by saying that, in case of a work emergency, they might need to get back to the office quickly and commercial air travel could hinder that. Firms that include this as a perk for their executives, according to one set of research, under-perform against the market average in terms of shareholder returns by about 4% each Of course, the real reason some companies might have private jets is not because it makes economic sense, because it quite often doesn’t. It’s because the people who decide whether the firm will use these are the very people that will use them. In many instances, the explanation is not economic, it’s societal.   11. @jeremyleonbarlow: The economics are complex. I mean if you are living in Los Angeles and you have 6 or 7 buddies and you all want to fly to Vegas for the weekend, you can rent a light jet to fly you all collectively to Vegas and back for about the same cost as a commercial round trip ticket, give or take a couple of hundred dollars each per ticket. I mean the complexity of commercial ticket pricing gives a lot of wiggle room, but a realistic rental price for 8 friends flying LA to Vegas round trip on a light jet is between $500 and $600 each.

Renting a long range heavy jet or super mid-size private jet for a lengthy trip between cities for a business isn't just about the time savings for a single person in management typically. I mean a 15 or 20 seat private business jet probably has at a minimum ten people flying on it, so you need to factor in the price differential between X number of tickets and the costs of chartering or using a company owned jet, and what is more given fixed costs of an aircraft, even with high up front costs, owning a jet might be more economical still. This is because there are ways to improve the value of an owned aircraft which allows you to factor more than just the value of those folks' time savings at aurports. You must add to that calculus the fact that many businesses and individuals also subsidize company or personal travel on a private jet by triple net leasing their owned jet to a charter service with a deal that allows them 10 or more hours per month of usage at cost. This type of leasing arrangement is often through a service that allows them to use a network of jets in the charter service's fleet. This helps obscure travel by the company, protecting proprietary information in many instances better than traditional private ownership. I mean Taylor Swift and Elon Musk's tail numbers were being tracked as I recall and Sergei Brin and Larry Page decided chartering was more economical for this exact reason. Competitors knowing your next move by tracking where you flew is not a good thing, but owning and using a networked leaseback arrangement can be more economical in addition to obscuring your travel.

These leasing arrangements maximize cash flow on the jet as an asset to both the charter operator and the lessor by allowing the jets to keep moving and avoid a fixed base of operation. This maximizes charter revenue. This reduces the costs further for the owner of the jet by spreading fixed costs over more hours of usage and offloading those costs onto chartered guests. I mean with amortization and depreciation, a company which might have spent $7.5 million flying private over the course of five years, after the savings associated with the right charter leaseback arrangement, could have in fact spent just $360k for the same 600 hours of flight time. At what becomes $600 per hour for the flight time in such an arrangement, owning a private company jet makes a whole lot more sense.

Now it isn't always going to allow the company or individual to reduce costs that much, as some unexpected maintenance items might raise costs. I mean one cracked windshield could costs tens of thousands of dollars to replace, not to mention lost revenue from the loss of flight time while waiting for the repair, but it limits downside of ownership, and can still make flying private far more economical than traditional chartering. Of course chartering still might be cost effective for businesses or individuals who fly less frequently because of the value of time savings alone. This is why high usage owners can subsidize their travel costs.

- Federal Air Surgeon on Pilot Mental Health: Kind: captions Language: en-US Hi, I'm Dr. Susan Northrup, the FAA's federal air surgeon. You may have seen a lot of focus recently on pilot mental health. I want you to know that the FAA is committed to prioritizing the mental health of pilots. We are doing everything we can to encourage open discussion of these issues and early intervention when necessary. Most conditions, if treated, do not disqualify a pilot from flying. In fact, only about 0.1% of all medical certificate applicants who disclose health issues are final denied. In numerous venues, we have tried to decrease the stigma in the aviation community. Just as society grapples with further reducing the stigma surrounding mental health through our educational and outreach efforts, we have attempted to dispel the myths. A mental health diagnosis is not a career ender. Any pilot with a substance use problem who achieves satisfactory recovery will get back in the cockpit. And we continue to look for ways to further break down other barriers. We have hired additional mental health professionals to expand our in-house expertise and to decrease wait times for return to fly decisions. We have completed clinical research and amended policy to make it much easier for pilots on a widening number of antidepressants to continue with their careers. We have streamlined the ability for pilots with minor or remote ADHD or A.D.D., post-traumatic stress disorder and situational depression or adjustment disorders to obtain an airman medical certificate. We have increased mental health training for aviation medical examiners to enable them to better help pilots. And we make sure to update the checklists and instructions in our Guide for Aviation Medical Examiners each month. Ultimately, though, any discussion about safety and performance must involve open and honest communication. During the periodic medical examination, pilots must disclose all existing physical and psychological conditions and medications, and the aviation medical examiner should follow up on each of these appropriately. There are times when safety dictates that we must disqualify a pilot. But I want to stress that the FAA is working hard to make these cases the exception and not the rule. We have advocated for and been closely involved with pilot peer support programs throughout the industry. We work closely with pilot advocacy groups and universities to develop similar programs for commercial and business aviation, as well as general aviation. As with any other medical issue, early treatment for mental health conditions almost always results in a better outcome. We look forward to working together with you on our shared goal of making every flight a safe one. Thank you.

  1. @ashleighhennessy [6 likes]: HOW TO FIX THIS IN 1 WEEK - Hi anyone who's struggling with this. I'm the 1 in 5 - I have this constant hunger but I'm able to stay slim (my BMI is 21). Here are my strategies: When I've been binging for a few weeks or even months, with uncontrollable hunger, I start where I am. I think about (or write down) what im eating, just the meal / snack, how frequently I'm eating. Usually at my worst it'll be pretty constant, every half hour or hour. So then I search youtube for "destress tolerance skills". It usually comes up with strategies, distraction, stiting with your emotions, havign a walk, a hot bath, light a candle etc, workout. Distractions. So then I make a note of the techniques I wanna use, and then on Day 1 of Binge Recovery I start by eating how frequently I was, allowing myself to eat whatever foods i want but not more than 1 bowl/plate etc. At my worst, this is usually every half hour or every hour. So i eat something every hour or half hour, just somethnig small. I try and make some of these proper meals/snacks, and some what i call 'low calorie fillers', so things like cucumber sticks, diet soda etc. if I can do thata nd cope with not eating for a bit longer. And then in the time in between my next meal/snacks, where im stressed and feeling very tense and thinking about food constantly, I use the distress tolerance skills i chose (10 minute workout, walk listening to my favourite songs, doom scrolling) and tell myself its just e.g. 20 more minutes till i can eat again, i can do it. And then on Day 2, I space the meals/snacks out to every hour. Then on Day 3 every 1-2hrs as I need. At this point i real;ly start trying to increase the nutritional value of the food, so less junk annd more healthy foods. Then by day 3, I notice im getting better at sitting with my emotions, recognising when im hungry vs stressed. I tell myself 'im anxious' rather than thinking 'im hungry'. Your thoughts become real, they have power. Then by day 5 I can go 2-3hrs without eating, or if im very focused on my work i go every 3-4hrs. By day 6, im no longer thinking constantly about food and i feel much better, stomach has shrunk and im feeling good about myself. Sometimes I have a coffee or tea at the end of my meal - I sytart to assocatiate that coffee with ending eating, so it starts making me not hungry (this is classical conditioning, you can train your brain to associate certain things with not feeling hungry). The workouts i do you can find by just searching youtube e.g. '10 minute beginner abs workout' or whatever body part you feel like. Also check out 'The Binge Eating Therapist' on youtube, her strategies have helped me when nothing else worked. She talks about coming to terms with the grief of ending a meal when you want more, accepting that you want to eat more but dont have to act on that impulse. Let me know if this helps anyone! Im on day 3 of my current recovery right now.

- How to Turn off the Fight/Flight/Freeze Response: Anxiety Skills #4: Kind: captions Language: en I'd like to do a quick introduction into grounding activities Being grounded means that you are in your body, you're feeling your emotions and you're aware of your present moment experience. It also means that you're feeling responsible for your safety and well-being. Grounding is an important therapeutic approach for managing anxiety, flashbacks, symptoms of post traumatic stress disorder and for improving your mental health. Grounding strengthens your parasympathetic response this is your body's natural ability to return to calm. This parasympathetic response counteracts the fight, flight or freeze response. PTSD and other anxiety disorders are disordered in their essence because you're feeling like you're in danger when you're actually safe. So your body's having this danger response this fight flight freeze response when you're present moment experience is actually safe and that's one reason why they are disordered. Anxiety is often about worries about the future- what might happen. And depression is often about discouragement about the past. And both of these can be counteracted with an increase in our ability to be here in the present moment. Most of the time our present moment is a safe and secure environment if it's not we need to leave so we need to create a safe and secure environment in our present moment. But grounding activities help us activate that safe feeling in our brain by reminding ourselves that here in the present moment we are safe. When we're grounded and calm were able to work through emotions and memories in a healthy way. Our body has a natural ability to resolve trauma or to work through emotions, but when we're panicked or in our fight flight freeze danger response our brain shuts down those abilities because it thinks that our survival is at stake. We can train ourselves to trigger this calming response by practicing grounding activities. It's important to practice these exercises over and over again so that they become habitual and we can draw on them in moments of stress or anxiety. Many of the grounding activities that I'm going to teach you use your body as a way to create that sense of calm so these are physiological grounding skills. This is called a bottom-up approach bottom-up meaning we go from the body to the brain instead of from the brain to itself and this makes sense because most of us aren't able to think ourselves calm. Like if you're stressed out and you say "Don't be anxious! Don't be anxious!" that like never works we can never actually tell ourselves to calm down by just forcing it from the top down. That's partly because our brain, when it feels threatened, it reacts at a very animalistic level it's using this deep part of our brain in the limbic system and the brainstem to react impulsively or instinctively to danger. So this makes a lot of sense this is a really functional reaction. So if a tiger jumps out at you you're not going to be using your thinking part of your brain to be like "Hmm I wonder what I should do with this?" your body's gonna naturally and instinctively gonna run away. So it's gonna have this physiological reaction this fight flight freeze response. It turns up your heart rate and it turns up your breathing rate. Since we can't access those deeper survival parts of our brain with our thoughts the best way to access that calm is through our body. So this is called a bottom-up approach to grounding. Our five senses anchor us to our body and our surroundings. Using this bottom-up approach is a much more effective way to treat anxiety and stress than trying to simply change how we think. And by creating that physiological calm we're able to lay the foundation for future cognitive work. Many of these activities focus on our breathing and that's because our breathing is something that happens both automatically and we have some degree of control over so that can help trigger that change in the limbic system that we need to create that physiological calm. Grounding exercises help connect us to our body and help us reassert that sense of self control and they reorient us to the safe context that we're actually in in the present moment. One of the ways that we gain confidence in our ability to use these grounding exercises is by doing a check-in before and after the exercise and one way to do that is to do a quick scale. So before you do a grounding exercise scale your anxiety on a scale from zero to ten and then do your grounding activity and then just check in with yourself again afterwards and put yourself again on the scale from zero to ten. Now it's not necessary for you to decrease your anxiety every time. Sometimes a grounding activity is just about increasing your awareness and your contact with the present moment. But if you do these consistently you will notice a gradual pattern of decreasing your anxiety little by little. We need to be able to notice these small victories because they can add up to large gains over time. Another way you can look at the effects of these grounding activities is to ask yourself these questions before and after: What are you noticing in your body? What are you noticing with your breath? What's happening with your feelings? What's happening with your heart? and What's happening with your thoughts? During these grounding activities if at any point you need to take a break that's okay you can just pause the activity and then come back to it later. When you're initially learning these grounding exercises the best way to practice is when you're in a place that's quiet peaceful and calm. Don't wait until you're stressed out or in a chaotic environment to try them. And again it's important to practice these activities every day for a few weeks before trying to practice them in a stressful environment. The more you practice them the more habitual and instinctual they'll become and you can actually train yourself to calm your body down with just a thought or a word. As you get better and better at grounding you'll increase your confidence and ability to create that physiological calm even when you're in a stressful situation and you can gradually use your skills in more and more difficult situations to maintain that sense of calm that we all need to feel safe and happy. Over the next couple of months I'm making a bunch of grounding videos I hope they're helpful for you. Take time to practice them and please like, comment and subscribe. Thanks for watching and take care!

- Reduce Anxiety & Stress with the Physiological Sigh | Huberman Lab Quantal Clip: Kind: captions Language: en-US [dramatic music] - Billions of people suffer from stress and there are tools to combat stress that involve things like meditation, breath work, good nutrition, good social connections and avoiding all bad things in life. And while those are powerful, the problem is, they require that people step away from the stress inducing activity. By contrast, my lab and other laboratories have been very interested in developing tools that allow us to push back on stress. In other words, feel more calm in real time, meaning without having to disengage The best way that I am aware to do that is called the physiological sigh. A physiological sigh is a pattern of breathing that involves two inhales followed by an extended exhale. Physiological sighs were discovered in the 1930s, as a pattern of breathing that people go into spontaneously when they're in claustrophobic environments or in deep sleep, when there's a buildup of a gas called carbon dioxide in the blood stream. Carbon dioxide triggers the impulse to breathe. There are neurons in the brain that know when carbon dioxide levels have gotten too high. And when the levels get too high, they trigger inhale and exhale, or double inhale and exhale. Now you can do physiological sighs voluntarily, anytime you're feeling too stressed and you want to feel more calm. You do it like this. [breathing] So it's a double inhale. And typically, the first inhale is longer than the second but the second one is still important to do. And then a very long, extended exhale. Typically, both inhales are through the nose and the exhale is through the mouth. That's the most effective way to do the physiological sigh. However, you can't breathe through your nose or your mouth for whatever reason do it all through your mouth or all through your nose. The second inhale is really important because your lungs are not just two big bags of air. They're two big bags of air with lots of little sacks, millions of sacks. And if you were to lay out those sacks, their volume is as big as a tennis court. And that allows both the intake of more oxygen but also the offload of carbon dioxide. So when you do the double inhale, it reinflates any of these little sacks that have collapsed and in doing so, it allows you to offload more carbon dioxide. So if you're feeling stressed in any circumstance, inhale twice through the nose and then exhale long through the mouth, if you want, you can repeat it a second or even a third time, but typically, just one or two, maybe three physiological sighs are sufficient to bring your level of stress and alertness down very fast and allow you to feel more calm.

- The Race To Stay Profitable In The Air Industry | CNBC Marathon: Kind: captions Language: en The airline industry is a long-cycle business. Spirit has had one challenge after another coming out of the pandemic. The icon of budget air travel in the U.S. has not been profitable since 2019, and it lost over $2 billion since 2020. Airlines that have shown more success have greater exposure to premium and corporate. The biggest competitor that United has right now is Delta. United very much wants to rule the world. But when it comes to profitability, it's still chasing that number one spot. Everybody's dealing with the fact that costs are 30% higher than they were in 2019. Some airlines decided to place orders with Airbus for aircraft rather than Boeing. ...Which has been dealing with crisis after crisis. Airbus has profited from the challenges that Boeing has faced. We're really talking about a battle for the 2030s here. The 2020s have been decided. Airbus and Boeing have been competing head-to-head for decades as the world's largest commercial airplane makers. The European-based Airbus entered the market 56 years after its rival. And today, 76% of the world's fleet is made up of Airbus and Boeing planes. But over the last few years, Airbus has edged out Boeing for market dominance. Airbus is clearly in the lead and they have a good lead. And if you just want to measure that, it's by backlog of the airplanes. Airbus has made a steady profit since the pandemic. While Boeing hasn't made a profit since 2018. Airbus total backlog, which are planes that have been ordered but not yet delivered, is 8,726, compared to Boeing's 5,643, which represents billions of dollars in revenue and is estimated to take almost 13 years to complete. We've gone from back in 1978 where you would hardly ever see an Airbus airplane in the U.S., to now having well over 50% of the market here in the U.S. Since 2019, Airbus has delivered more planes annually than Boeing, which has been dealing with crisis after crisis, including two deadly crashes of its best-selling 737 Max eight, which grounded that aircraft for nearly two years. It's also been dealing with significant financial losses, manufacturing issues and production limitations. One thing I want to be clear about: Airbus was already winning the race before the Max ran into trouble because the A321 in general was winning share. Narrowbodies make up the majority of the commercial aircraft flying today. These planes are very versatile. They can do multiple short trips a day or across oceans and continents. Boeing 737 was the world's best-selling narrow-body until 2019, when Airbus A320 surpassed it in total orders. And depending on how many planes are delivered in 2025, the A320 could take the reins as the most produced jet family in history. If you look across the board at the backlogs and indeed at orders, everything is fine in every category except for the A321, which is absolutely crushing it. CNBC explores how Airbus came to be the biggest commercial plane manufacturer and whether it can stay on top. The official 737 christening ceremony took place in the new final assembly building. In the late '60s, Boeing was dominating the commercial aircraft market. It had just debuted the 737 and the world's first ever wide-body jet. The massive 747 European aerospace companies took notice. The biggest misconception about Airbus is that it was some sort of instant creation, circa 1969. It really wasn't. It was a merger of a number of European national champion aerospace companies that had been building planes since World War Two or World War One, and the only way they could do it was by pooling their resources: thus Airbus. As more people took to the skies, airlines looked to operate bigger aircraft. At the time, all wide-body planes had 3 or 4 engines. For reference, a wide-body typically has two aisles, flies longer distances, and can carry anywhere from 250 to 500 passengers. While a narrow-body or single aisle has one single aisle through, the cabin flies shorter distances and can have 100 to 230 seats. Airbus shows up in the 1970s saying, "Hey, we have a wide-body plane, but with just two engines." And airlines sat up and said, "Whoa, two engines..." And obviously two engines that can take you the same distance as three are better because your maintenance costs are a lot lower. In 1972, Airbus introduced the first twin-engine wide-body jetliner: the A300. Despite having only two engines, the A300 could only carry around 250 passengers versus the massive 747, which could be configured to fit over 500. Not a lot of people saw the need for an intermediate sized yet. So, production rates were 1 or 2 per month for quite a few years. In 1978, the Deregulation Act was enacted in the U.S., creating more competition, new airlines and expansion of smaller ones. As airline needs shifted in the '80s, the demand for cost-effective, short-haul aircraft grew. They began to see the need for a new-generation single aisle, basically centered around 150 seats. Airbus had not yet made a profit, and the company needed a plane in its fleet to compete with Boeing's popular narrow-body, the 737, which had already been around since 1967. Airbus launched the A320 single aisle program in 1984. The workhorse of the Airbus commercial jet family is the A320. The A320 was one of the few commercial aircraft designed to compete against Boeing's 737 powerhouse, and has succeeded where others have failed. Its fuselage is a little bit wider than the 737, and some airlines have taken advantage of that to put in slightly wider seats. The A320 debuted with a lot of new advanced technology. It was one of the first commercial jets that used a side stick versus yoke controls. This is still a key difference between Airbus and Boeing planes today. It also introduced fly-by-wire controls, meaning when you pull back on the side stick, it sends electronic signals to a computer to move the aircraft versus a physical cable. One could argue from a flight control's point of view, the A320 is a generation ahead of the 737. When it first came into the market, it was controversial. Fly-by-wire when it first came out into the market was something that was really just used in military airplanes. The jet's debut, however, ended in a crash during the first demonstration flight at an airshow in 1988. When the A320 was first introduced, it suffered a series of accidents, including some fatal accidents that killed crew and passengers, and that really stalled the public acceptance of the plane. It took a while there, too, to get traction, but when it did, well, it succeeded far beyond expectations. A320 just came into the world 20 years later and has that advantage in terms of just fundamental design differences to the airframe. By the 1990s, the A320 success would start to put pressure on its U.S. competitors. Airbus, which was backed by European governments, made its first operating profits. Once Airbus was more market-proven as an aircraft builder and U.S. airlines started to see the performance results of the Airbus aircraft in other airlines' fleets, they became more open to buying Airbus planes, and it was really the A320 that helped Airbus crack the door opened into the U.S. market and many other parts of the world. Basically, Boeing through all of this had 50% of the market. So, what you basically had was Douglas, Lockheed and Airbus duking it out for the other half. Lockheed exited sometime in the mid-80s, and then everyone knew McDonnell Douglas. Matter of time. In 1997, McDonnell Douglas merged with Boeing, forming the duopoly we know today. If you think about the evolution of Airbus, I mean, Airbus really started in the narrow-body market with no market share, edged out Douglas, and then continued to get share. The Boeing 737 family and the Airbus A320 family are the company's best-selling planes, and have been a crucial segment of competition. The list price for one A321neo is around $129 million. Competition was head-to-head when it came to wide-bodies too. In the '90s, more fuel-efficient, twin-engine wide-bodies were introduced. Airbus debuted the A330 and 340, while Boeing introduced its 777. The A330, they kept refining it at Airbus. It did pretty well, and then Boeing attacked with the 787 Dreamliner. And despite all of the flaws baked into that development program, it did better than the A330 in the long run. Then, from 2003 to 2005, Airbus took more orders and delivered more planes than Boeing. That 50-50 formula kept persisting, and what Boeing was doing was effectively winning on profitability rather than market share. They were willing to say, "Alright, we're going to be 50%, but our margins are going to be 9% and yours are going to be 4%...or less." It was a formula that worked for them until, of course, the problems with the Max. Despite declining sales for four engine jets, Airbus spent billions creating the biggest double-decker quad-engine commercial aircraft in the world and possibly the most identifiable Airbus plane: the A380. It was in service from 2007 until it ended production in 2021. The A380 is an engineering marvel. Unfortunately, in a way, the A380's success was eclipsed by the success of Airbus's smaller aircraft, namely the A330 family and more recently, the A350, which are twin engine aircraft that can hold 250 to 400, 450 or so passengers and operate ultra-long range routes. And you looked at Airbus and you thought, "Wow, they're going to be tied for number one. You might even be number one. The only thing you could do to screw that up is launch a superjumbo for a market that's going away super fast." And that's exactly what they did. It cost them a decade. Today, Boeing still dominates the wide-body market with nearly 56% of the global fleet. These aircraft are used for long haul travel like international flights. Boeing currently has a backlog of over 1,500 wide-body planes. The company has had multiple manufacturing issues with its 787 Dreamliner and the 777X, which has yet to be certified, is years behind schedule. In 2012, Airbus announced it would begin manufacturing in Mobile, Alabama. You go back to 1978 and Airbus had a small office here in the U.S. with about a dozen people working there. Roll forward to 2025, and we have a very large manufacturing facility that manufactures airplanes in Mobile, Alabama. We build helicopters in Mississippi, we build satellites in Florida and actually are present in over 40 locations across the U.S. today. Airbus' biggest customer is Delta Airlines. It has slightly more Airbus planes in its fleet than Boeing. Airbus has had a different manufacturing strategy than Boeing. Theirs has been more regional, so they have a final assembly plant, a fowl, as they call them, in Asia, one in North America, one in Europe. Where Boeing's final assembly has been highly concentrated in North America, China was almost exclusively a Boeing customer for many, many years. By having a final assembly line in China, I think Airbus would argue that that helped them get some share in China. Airbus has been manufacturing in the U.S. for nearly ten years. It's about to open a third production line, two of which are for the 320 family and the other for its smaller 220 jets it acquired from Bombardier in In 2010, Airbus announced a new variation of its best-selling narrow-body called the A320neo, short for "new engine option," which promised a 15% less fuel burn than the previous generation. In 2011, Airbus had a record number of orders for 1,419 aircraft, more than double than the year before. That same year, American Airlines, which had been strictly a Boeing customer, put in an order for hundreds of these new planes. And in 2019, the A320 family surpassed the 737 in total orders. Originally, the business case was for around 600 airplanes, and now we are up to 19,000. Boeing's response to the more fuel-efficient A320 was the re-engined 737 Max that debuted in 2017. But over the next few years, the manufacturer faced a series of challenges. In 2018 and 2019, two 737 Max 8s crashed and killed 346 people. It was later found a flight control system known as MCAS had malfunctioned on both flights. The planes were grounded for several months and production rates were reduced. Then the pandemic brought air travel to a halt, and the company had a record loss of $12 billion in 2020. Airbus saw losses of over 1 billion in both 2019 and 2020, but returned to profitability in 2021, but Airbus has consecutively had more annual orders and deliveries since 2019, making it the world's largest planemaker. Then, in 2024, a door plug flew off a Boeing 737 Max 9 plane, leading to more production delays of its best-selling jet and an annual loss of $11.8 billion in 2024. Some airlines decided to place orders with Airbus for aircraft rather than Boeing. And if you look at Airbus' backlog today, it's dominated by A321. Airlines looking to cut down on fuel costs have been turning to these more efficient, longer range narrow-bodies for longer flights like JFK to LA or the East Coast to Europe. Of our single aisle order book or backlog as we call it, 72% of the Airbus A321, and that's opening up routes and markets that they couldn't fly directly before. The economics with single aisles were so much better than the economics with wide-bodies. And the Airbus A321neo wasn't perfect, but it was the biggest and best single aisle you could get, and leading Airbus to effectively a 60% or 65% market share from the standpoint of both the deliveries and orders and backlog. It's become the hinge factor between the two. Boeing doesn't really have a comparable airplane. 737 Max 9 isn't quite as big. The 10 is big, but it doesn't quite have the range. So, where Airbus has done well in the market is they have a product that Boeing really doesn't have. The 737 Max 10 has a range of 3,100 nautical miles and can seat a maximum of 230 people, versus the A321neo, which has a range of 4,700 nautical miles and can seat 244 passengers. But the Max 10 has yet to be certified. The bad news is, given these delays, these many year delays, and given the challenges, and also, frankly, you've got this attack on the administrative state in Washington, which is also toxic from Boeing's standpoint, these are resources they need to certify planes, and they're increasingly in short supply. So, there's a lot about DOGE and the attack on the FAA and other administrative agencies that's really not all good from the standpoint of Boeing's certification. In 2024, Airbus delivered 766 planes compared to Boeing's 348. Most of the revenue from aircraft is received on delivery. With all of these challenges facing questions remain about whether the low-cost model is dead or if it's just evolving. They are recognizing that they can appeal to a passenger that wants a little bit better experience, like extra legroom, maybe a little bit more service in-flight. So, they are recognizing that and trying to appeal to that and expand their customer base a little bit more. But in general, I don't think the low-cost carrier industry is dead. It just needs to be resized for the current realities.

addressing the elephant in the room opportunity cost by choosing a career in medicine of course you're missing out on all those other opportunities that would have been available to you had you chosen a different career path that's because to become a doctor in the united states you must complete four years of college then another four years of medical school followed by three to seven years of world he who understands it earns it he who doesn't pays it to demonstrate this point here's an example if you were to invest just 500 on the stock market every single month starting at the age of 25 and averaged a modest annual return of seven percent each year you'd have around 1.2 million dollars at the age of 65 despite only having invested 240 000 of your own money in contrast if you were to begin investing that same 500 a month at the age of 35 you would have less than half of that at around 567 000 this is the power of compound interest to afford college and medical school many students have to take out student loans as such very few students are able to build sizable savings and start investing during their early 20s in fact many don't even start seriously investing until they're AI Overview
Governor Phil Murphy announced New Jersey's eighth and final round of medical debt relief, eliminating $100 million in debt for about 60,000 residents by partnering with Undue Medical Debt and using federal funds to purchase and erase these bills, continuing a program that has helped hundreds of thousands of New Jerseyans facing overwhelming medical expenses. Eligible individuals, identified automatically without applications, typically have incomes at or below 400% of the poverty level or debt exceeding 5% of their income, with relief coming via letters from Undue. ￼
Key Details:
Total Relief: An estimated $100 million in medical debt erased.
Beneficiaries: Approximately 60,000 New Jersey residents.
Partner: the national nonprofit Undue Medical Debt (formerly RIP Medical Debt).
Funding: Leftover federal funds from the American Rescue Plan.
How it Works: Undue Medical Debt buys portfolios of past-due medical debt from providers (like hospitals and collections agencies) for pennies on the dollar, then abolishes the debt.
Eligibility: No application needed; recipients are identified if they earn up to 400% of the federal poverty level or have debt equal to 5% or more of their annual income.
Notification: Beneficiaries receive letters from Undue Medical Debt. ￼
Context of the Program:
This is the final phase of a multi-round initiative that began in 2024, with earlier rounds targeting debts from specific providers like Prime Healthcare.
The state also enacted legislation, including the Louisa Carman Medical Debt Relief Act, to prevent medical debt from negatively impacting credit scores. ￼
This initiative provides significant financial relief, removing burdens for families and making healthcare more accessible, according to Governor Murphy. ￼
